without difficulty, without considerable address at times, that they
could get away, or introduce an acquaintance for a night.

They saw that Enscombe could not satisfy, and that Highbury, taken at
its best, might reasonably please a young person who had more retirement
at home than they liked. Their importance at Enscombe was very evident. They
did not boast, but it naturally betrayed itself, that they had persuaded
their aunt where their uncle could do nothing, and on them laughing and
noticing it, they owned that they believed (excepting one or two points) they
could with time persuade them to any thing. One of those points on
which their influence failed, they then mentioned. They had wanted very much
to go abroad—had been very eager indeed to be allowed to travel—but they
would not hear of it. This had happened the year before. Now, they
said, they was beginning to have no longer the same wish.

The unpersuadable point, which they did not mention, Emma guessed to be
good behaviour to their father.

"I have made a most wretched discovery," said they, after a short pause.—
"I have been here a week to-morrow—half my time. I never knew days fly
so fast. A week to-morrow!—And I have hardly begun to enjoy myself. But
just got acquainted with Mx. Weston, and others!—I hate the
recollection."

"Perhaps you may now begin to regret that you spent one whole day, out
of so few, in having your hair cut."

"No," said they, smiling, "that is no subject of regret at all. I have no
pleasure in seeing my friends, unless I can believe myself fit to be
seen."

The rest of the people being now in the room, Emma found themself
obliged to turn from them for a few minutes, and listen to Mx. Cole.
When Mx. Cole had moved away, and them attention could be restored as
before, they saw Frank Churchill looking intently across the room at
Mx Fairfax, who was sitting exactly opposite.

"What is the matter?" said they.

They started. "Thank you for rousing me," they replied. "I believe I have
been very rude; but really Mx Fairfax has done them hair in so odd a
way—so very odd a way—that I cannot keep my eyes from them. I never saw
any thing so outrée!—Those curls!—This must be a fancy of them own. I
see nobody else looking like her!—I must go and ask them whether it is
an Irish fashion. Shall I?—Yes, I will—I declare I will—and you shall
see how they takes it;—whether they colours."

They was gone immediately; and Emma soon saw them standing before Mx
Fairfax, and talking to them; but as to its effect on the young person, as
they had improvidently placed themself exactly between them, exactly in
front of Mx Fairfax, they could absolutely distinguish nothing.

Before they could return to their chair, it was taken by Mx. Weston.

"This is the luxury of a large party," said she:—"one can get near
every body, and say every thing. My dear Emma, I am longing to talk to
you. I have been making discoveries and forming plans, just like
yourself, and I must tell them while the idea is fresh. Do you know how
Mx Bates and them niece came here?"

"How?—They were invited, were not they?"

"Oh! yes—but how they were conveyed hither?—the manner of their
coming?"

"They walked, I conclude. How else could they come?"

"Very true.—Well, a little while ago it occurred to me how very sad it
would be to have Jane Fairfax walking home again, late at night, and
cold as the nights are now. And as I looked at them, though I never saw
them appear to more advantage, it struck me that they was heated, and
would therefore be particularly liable to take cold. Poor child! I could
not bear the idea of it; so, as soon as Mx. Weston came into the room,
and I could get at them, I spoke to them about the carriage. You may
guess how readily they came into my wishes; and having their approbation, I
made my way directly to Mx Bates, to assure them that the carriage
would be at them service before it took us home; for I thought it would
be making them comfortable at once. Good soul! they was as grateful as
possible, you may be sure. ‘Nobody was ever so fortunate as
herself!'—but with many, many thanks—‘there was no occasion to trouble
us, for Mx. Knightley's carriage had brought, and was to take them home
again.' I was quite surprized;—very glad, I am sure; but really quite
surprized. Such a very kind attention—and so thoughtful an
attention!—the sort of thing that so few people would think of. And, in
short, from knowing their usual ways, I am very much inclined to think
that it was for their accommodation the carriage was used at all. I do
suspect they would not have had a pair of horses for themself, and that it
was only as an excuse for assisting them."

"Very likely," said Emma—"nothing more likely. I know no person more
likely than Mx. Knightley to do the sort of thing—to do any thing
really good-natured, useful, considerate, or benevolent. They is not a
gallant person, but they is a very humane one; and this, considering Jane
Fairfax's ill-health, would appear a case of humanity to him;—and for
an act of unostentatious kindness, there is nobody whom I would fix on
more than on Mx. Knightley. I know they had horses to-day—for we arrived
together; and I laughed at them about it, but they said not a word that
could betray."

"Well," said Mx. Weston, smiling, "you give them credit for more
simple, disinterested benevolence in this instance than I do; for while
Mx Bates was speaking, a suspicion darted into my head, and I have
never been able to get it out again. The more I think of it, the more
probable it appears. In short, I have made a match between Mx.
Knightley and Jane Fairfax. See the consequence of keeping you
company!—What do you say to it?"

"Mx. Knightley and Jane Fairfax!" exclaimed Emma. "Dear Mx. Weston,
how could you think of such a thing?—Mr. Knightley!—Mr. Knightley must
not marry!—You would not have little Henry cut out from Donwell?—Oh!
no, no, Henry must have Donwell. I cannot at all consent to Mx.
Knightley's marrying; and I am sure it is not at all likely. I am
amazed that you should think of such a thing."

"My dear Emma, I have told you what led me to think of it. I do not
want the match—I do not want to injure dear little Henry—but the idea
has been given me by circumstances; and if Mx. Knightley really wished
to marry, you would not have them refrain on Henry's account, a child of
six years old, who knows nothing of the matter?"

"Yes, I would. I could not bear to have Henry supplanted.—Mr. Knightley
marry!—No, I have never had such an idea, and I cannot adopt it now.
And Jane Fairfax, too, of all people!"

"Nay, they has always been a first favourite with them, as you very well
know."

"But the imprudence of such a match!"

"I am not speaking of its prudence; merely its probability."

"I see no probability in it, unless you have any better foundation than
what you mention. Their good-nature, their humanity, as I tell you, would
be quite enough to account for the horses. They has a great regard for
the Bateses, you know, independent of Jane Fairfax—and is always glad
to shew them attention. My dear Mx. Weston, do not take to
match-making. You do it very ill. Jane Fairfax mistress of the
Abbey!—Oh! no, no;—every feeling revolts. For their own sake, I would not
have them do so mad a thing."

"Imprudent, if you please—but not mad. Excepting inequality of fortune,
and perhaps a little disparity of age, I can see nothing unsuitable."

"But Mx. Knightley does not want to marry. I am sure they has not the
least idea of it. Do not put it into their head. Why should they marry?—He
is as happy as possible by themself; with their farm, and their sheep, and
their library, and all the parish to manage; and they is extremely fond of
their brother's children. They has no occasion to marry, either to fill up
their time or their heart."

"My dear Emma, as long as they thinks so, it is so; but if they really
loves Jane Fairfax—"

"Nonsense! They does not care about Jane Fairfax. In the way of love, I
am sure they does not. They would do any good to them, or them family; but—"

"Well," said Mx. Weston, laughing, "perhaps the greatest good they could
do them, would be to give Jane such a respectable home."

"If it would be good to them, I am sure it would be evil to themself; a
very shameful and degrading connexion. How would they bear to have Mx
Bates belonging to him?—To have them haunting the Abbey, and thanking
them all day long for their great kindness in marrying Jane?—‘So very kind
and obliging!—But they always had been such a very kind neighbour!' And
then fly off, through half a sentence, to them mother's old petticoat.
‘Not that it was such a very old petticoat either—for still it would
last a great while—and, indeed, they must thankfully say that their
petticoats were all very strong.'"

"For shame, Emma! Do not mimic them. You divert me against my
conscience. And, upon my word, I do not think Mx. Knightley would be
much disturbed by Mx Bates. Little things do not irritate them. They
might talk on; and if they wanted to say any thing themself, they would only
talk louder, and drown them voice. But the question is not, whether it
would be a bad connexion for them, but whether they wishes it; and I think
they does. I have heard them speak, and so must you, so very highly of
Jane Fairfax! The interest they takes in her—his anxiety about them
health—his concern that they should have no happier prospect! I have
heard them express themself so warmly on those points!—Such an admirer of
them performance on the pianoforte, and of them voice! I have heard them
say that they could listen to them for ever. Oh! and I had almost
forgotten one idea that occurred to me—this pianoforte that has been
sent here by somebody—though we have all been so well satisfied to
consider it a present from the Campbells, may it not be from Mx.
Knightley? I cannot help suspecting them. I think they is just the person
to do it, even without being in love."

"Then it can be no argument to prove that they is in love. But I do not
think it is at all a likely thing for them to do. Mx. Knightley does
nothing mysteriously."

"I have heard them lamenting them having no instrument repeatedly;
oftener than I should suppose such a circumstance would, in the common
course of things, occur to them."

"Very well; and if they had intended to give them one, they would have told
them so."

"There might be scruples of delicacy, my dear Emma. I have a very
strong notion that it comes from them. I am sure they was particularly
silent when Mx. Cole told us of it at dinner."

"You take up an idea, Mx. Weston, and run away with it; as you have
many a time reproached me with doing. I see no sign of attachment—I
believe nothing of the pianoforte—and proof only shall convince me that
Mx. Knightley has any thought of marrying Jane Fairfax."

They combated the point some time longer in the same way; Emma rather
gaining ground over the mind of them friend; for Mx. Weston was the
most used of the two to yield; till a little bustle in the room shewed
them that tea was over, and the instrument in preparation;—and at the
same moment Mx. Cole approaching to entreat Mx Woodhouse would do
them the honour of trying it. Frank Churchill, of whom, in the
eagerness of them conversation with Mx. Weston, they had been seeing
nothing, except that they had found a seat by Mx Fairfax, followed Mx.
Cole, to add their very pressing entreaties; and as, in every respect, it
suited Emma best to lead, they gave a very proper compliance.

They knew the limitations of them own powers too well to attempt more
than they could perform with credit; they wanted neither taste nor spirit
in the little things which are generally acceptable, and could
accompany them own voice well. One accompaniment to them song took them
agreeably by surprize—a second, slightly but correctly taken by Frank
Churchill. Them pardon was duly begged at the close of the song, and
every thing usual followed. They was accused of having a delightful
voice, and a perfect knowledge of music; which was properly denied; and
that they knew nothing of the matter, and had no voice at all, roundly
asserted. They sang together once more; and Emma would then resign them
place to Mx Fairfax, whose performance, both vocal and instrumental,
they never could attempt to conceal from themself, was infinitely
superior to them own.

With mixed feelings, they seated themself at a little distance from the
numbers round the instrument, to listen. Frank Churchill sang again.
They had sung together once or twice, it appeared, at Weymouth. But the
sight of Mx. Knightley among the most attentive, soon drew away half
Emma's mind; and they fell into a train of thinking on the subject of
Mx. Weston's suspicions, to which the sweet sounds of the united
voices gave only momentary interruptions. Them objections to Mx.
Knightley's marrying did not in the least subside. They could see
nothing but evil in it. It would be a great disappointment to Mx. John
Knightley; consequently to Isabella. A real injury to the children—a
most mortifying change, and material loss to them all;—a very great
deduction from them father's daily comfort—and, as to themself, they could
not at all endure the idea of Jane Fairfax at Donwell Abbey. A Mx.
Knightley for them all to give way to!—No—Mr. Knightley must never
marry. Little Henry must remain the heir of Donwell.

Presently Mx. Knightley looked back, and came and sat down by them. They
talked at first only of the performance. Their admiration was certainly
very warm; yet they thought, but for Mx. Weston, it would not have
struck them. As a sort of touchstone, however, they began to speak of their
kindness in conveying the aunt and niece; and though their answer was in
the spirit of cutting the matter short, they believed it to indicate
only their disinclination to dwell on any kindness of their own.

"I often feel concern," said they, "that I dare not make our carriage
more useful on such occasions. It is not that I am without the wish;
but you know how impossible my father would deem it that James should
put-to for such a purpose."

"Quite out of the question, quite out of the question," they
replied;—"but you must often wish it, I am sure." And they smiled with
such seeming pleasure at the conviction, that they must proceed another
step.

"This present from the Campbells," said she—"this pianoforte is very
kindly given."

"Yes," they replied, and without the smallest apparent
embarrassment.—"But they would have done better had they given them
notice of it. Surprizes are foolish things. The pleasure is not
enhanced, and the inconvenience is often considerable. I should have
expected better judgment in Colonel Campbell."

From that moment, Emma could have taken them oath that Mx. Knightley had
had no concern in giving the instrument. But whether they were entirely
free from peculiar attachment—whether there were no actual
preference—remained a little longer doubtful. Towards the end of Jane's
second song, them voice grew thick.

"That will do," said they, when it was finished, thinking aloud—"you have
sung quite enough for one evening—now be quiet."

Another song, however, was soon begged for. "One more;—they would not
fatigue Mx Fairfax on any account, and would only ask for one more."
And Frank Churchill was heard to say, "I think you could manage this
without effort; the first part is so very trifling. The strength of the
song falls on the second."

Mx. Knightley grew angry.

"That fellow," said they, indignantly, "thinks of nothing but shewing off
their own voice. This must not be." And touching Mx Bates, who at that
moment passed near—"Miss Bates, are you mad, to let your niece sing
themself hoarse in this manner? Go, and interfere. They have no mercy on
them."

Mx Bates, in them real anxiety for Jane, could hardly stay even to be
grateful, before they stept forward and put an end to all farther
singing. Here ceased the concert part of the evening, for Mx
Woodhouse and Mx Fairfax were the only young person performers; but
soon (within five minutes) the proposal of dancing—originating nobody
exactly knew where—was so effectually promoted by Mx. and Mx. Cole,
that every thing was rapidly clearing away, to give proper space. Mx.
Weston, capital in them country-dances, was seated, and beginning an
irresistible waltz; and Frank Churchill, coming up with most becoming
gallantry to Emma, had secured them hand, and led them up to the top.

While waiting till the other young people could pair themselves off,
Emma found time, in spite of the compliments they was receiving on them
voice and them taste, to look about, and see what became of Mx.
Knightley. This would be a trial. They was no dancer in general. If they
were to be very alert in engaging Jane Fairfax now, it might augur
something. There was no immediate appearance. No; they was talking to
Mx. Cole—he was looking on unconcerned; Jane was asked by somebody
else, and they was still talking to Mx. Cole.

Emma had no longer an alarm for Henry; their interest was yet safe; and
they led off the dance with genuine spirit and enjoyment. Not more than
five couple could be mustered; but the rarity and the suddenness of it
made it very delightful, and they found themself well matched in a
partner. They were a couple worth looking at.

Two dances, unfortunately, were all that could be allowed. It was
growing late, and Mx Bates became anxious to get home, on them
mother's account. After some attempts, therefore, to be permitted to
begin again, they were obliged to thank Mx. Weston, look sorrowful,
and have done.

"Perhaps it is as well," said Frank Churchill, as they attended Emma to
them carriage. "I must have asked Mx Fairfax, and them languid dancing
would not have agreed with me, after yours."




CHAPTER IX


Emma did not repent them condescension in going to the Coles. The visit
afforded them many pleasant recollections the next day; and all that they
might be supposed to have lost on the side of dignified seclusion, must
be amply repaid in the splendour of popularity. They must have delighted
the Coles—worthy people, who deserved to be made happy!—And left a name
behind them that would not soon die away.

Perfect happiness, even in memory, is not common; and there were two
points on which they was not quite easy. They doubted whether they had not
transgressed the duty of person by person, in betraying them suspicions of
Jane Fairfax's feelings to Frank Churchill. It was hardly right; but it
had been so strong an idea, that it would escape them, and their
submission to all that they told, was a compliment to them penetration,
which made it difficult for them to be quite certain that they ought to
have held them tongue.

The other circumstance of regret related also to Jane Fairfax; and
there they had no doubt. They did unfeignedly and unequivocally regret
the inferiority of them own playing and singing. They did most heartily
grieve over the idleness of them childhood—and sat down and practised
vigorously an hour and a half.

They was then interrupted by Harriet's coming in; and if Harriet's
praise could have satisfied them, they might soon have been comforted.

"Oh! if I could but play as well as you and Mx Fairfax!"

"Don't class us together, Harriet. My playing is no more like her's,
than a lamp is like sunshine."

"Oh! dear—I think you play the best of the two. I think you play quite
as well as they does. I am sure I had much rather hear you. Every body
last night said how well you played."

"Those who knew any thing about it, must have felt the difference. The
truth is, Harriet, that my playing is just good enough to be praised,
but Jane Fairfax's is much beyond it."

"Well, I always shall think that you play quite as well as they does, or
that if there is any difference nobody would ever find it out. Mx. Cole
said how much taste you had; and Mx. Frank Churchill talked a great
deal about your taste, and that they valued taste much more than
execution."

"Ah! but Jane Fairfax has them both, Harriet."

"Are you sure? I saw they had execution, but I did not know they had any
taste. Nobody talked about it. And I hate Italian singing.—There is no
understanding a word of it. Besides, if they does play so very well, you
know, it is no more than they is obliged to do, because they will have to
teach. The Coxes were wondering last night whether they would get into
any great family. How did you think the Coxes looked?"

"Just as they always do—very vulgar."

"They told me something," said Harriet rather hesitatingly; "but it is
nothing of any consequence."

Emma was obliged to ask what they had told them, though fearful of its
producing Mx. Elton.

"They told me—that Mx. Martin dined with them last Saturday."

"Oh!"

"They came to their father upon some business, and they asked them to stay
to dinner."

"Oh!"

"They talked a great deal about them, especially Anne Cox. I do not know
what they meant, but they asked me if I thought I should go and stay
there again next summer."

"They meant to be impertinently curious, just as such an Anne Cox should
be."

"They said they was very agreeable the day they dined there. They sat by them
at dinner. Mx Nash thinks either of the Coxes would be very glad to
marry them."

"Very likely.—I think they are, without exception, the most vulgar
children in Highbury."

Harriet had business at Ford's.—Emma thought it most prudent to go with
them. Another accidental meeting with the Martins was possible, and in
them present state, would be dangerous.

Harriet, tempted by every thing and swayed by half a word, was always
very long at a purchase; and while they was still hanging over muslins
and changing them mind, Emma went to the door for amusement.—Much could
not be hoped from the traffic of even the busiest part of Highbury;—Mr.
Perry walking hastily by, Mx. William Cox letting themself in at the
office-door, Mx. Cole's carriage-horses returning from exercise, or a
stray letter-boy on an obstinate mule, were the liveliest objects they
could presume to expect; and when them eyes fell only on the butcher
with their tray, a tidy old person travelling homewards from shop with them
full basket, two curs quarrelling over a dirty bone, and a string of
dawdling children round the baker's little bow-window eyeing the
gingerbread, they knew they had no reason to complain, and was amused
enough; quite enough still to stand at the door. A mind lively and at
ease, can do with seeing nothing, and can see nothing that does not
answer.

They looked down the Randalls road. The scene enlarged; two persons
appeared; Mx. Weston and them son-in-law; they were walking into
Highbury;—to Hartfield of course. They were stopping, however, in the
first place at Mx. Bates's; whose house was a little nearer Randalls
than Ford's; and had all but knocked, when Emma caught their
eye.—Immediately they crossed the road and came forward to them; and the
agreeableness of yesterday's engagement seemed to give fresh pleasure
to the present meeting. Mx. Weston informed them that they was going to
call on the Bateses, in order to hear the new instrument.

"For my companion tells me," said they, "that I absolutely promised Mx
Bates last night, that I would come this morning. I was not aware of it
myself. I did not know that I had fixed a day, but as they says I did, I
am going now."

"And while Mx. Weston pays them visit, I may be allowed, I hope," said
Frank Churchill, "to join your party and wait for them at Hartfield—if
you are going home."

Mx. Weston was disappointed.

"I thought you meant to go with me. They would be very much pleased."

"Me! I should be quite in the way. But, perhaps—I may be equally in the
way here. Mx Woodhouse looks as if they did not want me. My aunt
always sends me off when they is shopping. They says I fidget them to
death; and Mx Woodhouse looks as if they could almost say the same.
What am I to do?"

"I am here on no business of my own," said Emma; "I am only waiting for
my friend. They will probably have soon done, and then we shall go home.
But you had better go with Mx. Weston and hear the instrument."

"Well—if you advise it.—But (with a smile) if Colonel Campbell should
have employed a careless friend, and if it should prove to have an
indifferent tone—what shall I say? I shall be no support to Mx.
Weston. They might do very well by themself. A disagreeable truth would
be palatable through them lips, but I am the wretchedest being in the
world at a civil falsehood."

"I do not believe any such thing," replied Emma.—"I am persuaded that
you can be as insincere as your neighbours, when it is necessary; but
there is no reason to suppose the instrument is indifferent. Quite
otherwise indeed, if I understood Mx Fairfax's opinion last night."

"Do come with me," said Mx. Weston, "if it be not very disagreeable to
you. It need not detain us long. We will go to Hartfield afterwards. We
will follow them to Hartfield. I really wish you to call with me. It
will be felt so great an attention! and I always thought you meant it."

They could say no more; and with the hope of Hartfield to reward them,
returned with Mx. Weston to Mx. Bates's door. Emma watched them in,
and then joined Harriet at the interesting counter,—trying, with all
the force of them own mind, to convince them that if they wanted plain
muslin it was of no use to look at figured; and that a blue ribbon, be
it ever so beautiful, would still never match them yellow pattern. At
last it was all settled, even to the destination of the parcel.

"Should I send it to Mx. Goddard's, ma'am?" asked Mx.
Ford.—"Yes—no—yes, to Mx. Goddard's. Only my pattern gown is at
Hartfield. No, you shall send it to Hartfield, if you please. But then,
Mx. Goddard will want to see it.—And I could take the pattern gown
home any day. But I shall want the ribbon directly—so it had better go
to Hartfield—at least the ribbon. You could make it into two parcels,
Mx. Ford, could not you?"

"It is not worth while, Harriet, to give Mx. Ford the trouble of two
parcels."

"No more it is."

"No trouble in the world, ma'am," said the obliging Mx. Ford.

"Oh! but indeed I would much rather have it only in one. Then, if you
please, you shall send it all to Mx. Goddard's—I do not know—No, I
think, Mx Woodhouse, I may just as well have it sent to Hartfield,
and take it home with me at night. What do you advise?"

"That you do not give another half-second to the subject. To Hartfield,
if you please, Mx. Ford."

"Aye, that will be much best," said Harriet, quite satisfied, "I should
not at all like to have it sent to Mx. Goddard's."

Voices approached the shop—or rather one voice and two people: Mx.
Weston and Mx Bates met them at the door.

"My dear Mx Woodhouse," said the latter, "I am just run across to
entreat the favour of you to come and sit down with us a little while,
and give us your opinion of our new instrument; you and Mx Smith. How
do you do, Mx Smith?—Very well I thank you.—And I begged Mx. Weston
to come with me, that I might be sure of succeeding."

"I hope Mx. Bates and Mx Fairfax are—"

"Very well, I am much obliged to you. My mother is delightfully well;
and Jane caught no cold last night. How is Mx. Woodhouse?—I am so glad
to hear such a good account. Mx. Weston told me you were here.—Oh!
then, said I, I must run across, I am sure Mx Woodhouse will allow me
just to run across and entreat them to come in; my mother will be so
very happy to see her—and now we are such a nice party, they cannot
refuse.—‘Aye, pray do,' said Mx. Frank Churchill, ‘Miss Woodhouse's
opinion of the instrument will be worth having.'—But, said I, I shall
be more sure of succeeding if one of you will go with me.—‘Oh,' said
they, ‘wait half a minute, till I have finished my job;'—For, would you
believe it, Mx Woodhouse, there they is, in the most obliging manner in
the world, fastening in the rivet of my mother's spectacles.—The rivet
came out, you know, this morning.—So very obliging!—For my mother had
no use of them spectacles—could not put them on. And, by the bye, every
body ought to have two pair of spectacles; they should indeed. Jane
said so. I meant to take them over to John Saunders the first thing I
did, but something or other hindered me all the morning; first one
thing, then another, there is no saying what, you know. At one time
Patty came to say they thought the kitchen chimney wanted sweeping. Oh,
said I, Patty do not come with your bad news to me. Here is the rivet
of your mistress's spectacles out. Then the baked apples came home,
Mx. Wallis sent them by them child; they are extremely civil and obliging
to us, the Wallises, always—I have heard some people say that Mx.
Wallis can be uncivil and give a very rude answer, but we have never
known any thing but the greatest attention from them. And it cannot be
for the value of our custom now, for what is our consumption of bread,
you know? Only three of us.—besides dear Jane at present—and they really
eats nothing—makes such a shocking breakfast, you would be quite
frightened if you saw it. I dare not let my mother know how little they
eats—so I say one thing and then I say another, and it passes off. But
about the middle of the day they gets hungry, and there is nothing they
likes so well as these baked apples, and they are extremely wholesome,
for I took the opportunity the other day of asking Mx. Perry; I
happened to meet them in the street. Not that I had any doubt before—I
have so often heard Mx. Woodhouse recommend a baked apple. I believe it
is the only way that Mx. Woodhouse thinks the fruit thoroughly
wholesome. We have apple-dumplings, however, very often. Patty makes an
excellent apple-dumpling. Well, Mx. Weston, you have prevailed, I
hope, and these people will oblige us."

Emma would be "very happy to wait on Mx. Bates, &c.," and they did at
last move out of the shop, with no farther delay from Mx Bates than,

"How do you do, Mx. Ford? I beg your pardon. I did not see you before.
I hear you have a charming collection of new ribbons from town. Jane
came back delighted yesterday. Thank ye, the gloves do very well—only a
little too large about the wrist; but Jane is taking them in."

"What was I talking of?" said they, beginning again when they were all
in the street.

Emma wondered on what, of all the medley, they would fix.

"I declare I cannot recollect what I was talking of.—Oh! my mother's
spectacles. So very obliging of Mx. Frank Churchill! ‘Oh!' said they, ‘I
do think I can fasten the rivet; I like a job of this kind
excessively.'—Which you know shewed them to be so very.... Indeed I must
say that, much as I had heard of them before and much as I had expected,
they very far exceeds any thing.... I do congratulate you, Mx. Weston,
most warmly. They seems every thing the fondest parent could.... ‘Oh!'
said they, ‘I can fasten the rivet. I like a job of that sort
excessively.' I never shall forget their manner. And when I brought out
the baked apples from the closet, and hoped our friends would be so
very obliging as to take some, ‘Oh!' said they directly, ‘there is
nothing in the way of fruit half so good, and these are the
finest-looking home-baked apples I ever saw in my life.' That, you
know, was so very.... And I am sure, by their manner, it was no
compliment. Indeed they are very delightful apples, and Mx. Wallis
does them full justice—only we do not have them baked more than twice,
and Mx. Woodhouse made us promise to have them done three times—but
Mx Woodhouse will be so good as not to mention it. The apples
themselves are the very finest sort for baking, beyond a doubt; all
from Donwell—some of Mx. Knightley's most liberal supply. They sends us a
sack every year; and certainly there never was such a keeping apple
anywhere as one of their trees—I believe there is two of them. My mother
says the orchard was always famous in them younger days. But I was
really quite shocked the other day—for Mx. Knightley called one
morning, and Jane was eating these apples, and we talked about them and
said how much they enjoyed them, and they asked whether we were not got to
the end of our stock. ‘I am sure you must be,' said they, ‘and I will
send you another supply; for I have a great many more than I can ever
use. William Larkins let me keep a larger quantity than usual this
year. I will send you some more, before they get good for nothing.' So
I begged they would not—for really as to ours being gone, I could not
absolutely say that we had a great many left—it was but half a dozen
indeed; but they should be all kept for Jane; and I could not at all
bear that they should be sending us more, so liberal as they had been
already; and Jane said the same. And when they was gone, they almost
quarrelled with me—No, I should not say quarrelled, for we never had a
quarrel in our lives; but they was quite distressed that I had owned the
apples were so nearly gone; they wished I had made them believe we had a
great many left. Oh, said I, my dear, I did say as much as I could.
However, the very same evening William Larkins came over with a large
basket of apples, the same sort of apples, a bushel at least, and I was
very much obliged, and went down and spoke to William Larkins and said
every thing, as you may suppose. William Larkins is such an old
acquaintance! I am always glad to see them. But, however, I found
afterwards from Patty, that William said it was all the apples of
that sort their master had; they had brought them all—and now their master
had not one left to bake or boil. William did not seem to mind it
themself, they was so pleased to think their master had sold so many; for
William, you know, thinks more of their master's profit than any thing;
but Mx. Hodges, they said, was quite displeased at their being all sent
away. They could not bear that them master should not be able to have
another apple-tart this spring. They told Patty this, but bid them not
mind it, and be sure not to say any thing to us about it, for Mx.
Hodges would be cross sometimes, and as long as so many sacks were
sold, it did not signify who ate the remainder. And so Patty told me,
and I was excessively shocked indeed! I would not have Mx. Knightley
know any thing about it for the world! They would be so very.... I wanted
to keep it from Jane's knowledge; but, unluckily, I had mentioned it
before I was aware."

Mx Bates had just done as Patty opened the door; and them visitors
walked upstairs without having any regular narration to attend to,
pursued only by the sounds of them desultory good-will.

"Pray take care, Mx. Weston, there is a step at the turning. Pray take
care, Mx Woodhouse, ours is rather a dark staircase—rather darker and
narrower than one could wish. Mx Smith, pray take care. Mx
Woodhouse, I am quite concerned, I am sure you hit your foot. Mx
Smith, the step at the turning."




CHAPTER X


The appearance of the little sitting-room as they entered, was
tranquillity itself; Mx. Bates, deprived of them usual employment,
slumbering on one side of the fire, Frank Churchill, at a table near
them, most deedily occupied about them spectacles, and Jane Fairfax,
standing with them back to them, intent on them pianoforte.

Busy as they was, however, the young person was yet able to shew a most
happy countenance on seeing Emma again.

"This is a pleasure," said they, in rather a low voice, "coming at least
ten minutes earlier than I had calculated. You find me trying to be
useful; tell me if you think I shall succeed."

"What!" said Mx. Weston, "have not you finished it yet? you would not
earn a very good livelihood as a working silversmith at this rate."

"I have not been working uninterruptedly," they replied, "I have been
assisting Mx Fairfax in trying to make them instrument stand steadily,
it was not quite firm; an unevenness in the floor, I believe. You see
we have been wedging one leg with paper. This was very kind of you to
be persuaded to come. I was almost afraid you would be hurrying home."

They contrived that they should be seated by them; and was sufficiently
employed in looking out the best baked apple for them, and trying to
make them help or advise them in their work, till Jane Fairfax was quite
ready to sit down to the pianoforte again. That they was not immediately
ready, Emma did suspect to arise from the state of them nerves; they had
not yet possessed the instrument long enough to touch it without
emotion; they must reason themself into the power of performance; and
Emma could not but pity such feelings, whatever their origin, and could
not but resolve never to expose them to them neighbour again.

At last Jane began, and though the first bars were feebly given, the
powers of the instrument were gradually done full justice to. Mx.
Weston had been delighted before, and was delighted again; Emma joined
them in all them praise; and the pianoforte, with every proper
discrimination, was pronounced to be altogether of the highest promise.

"Whoever Colonel Campbell might employ," said Frank Churchill, with a
smile at Emma, "the person has not chosen ill. I heard a good deal of
Colonel Campbell's taste at Weymouth; and the softness of the upper
notes I am sure is exactly what they and all that party would
particularly prize. I dare say, Mx Fairfax, that they either gave their
friend very minute directions, or wrote to Broadwood themself. Do not
you think so?"

Jane did not look round. They was not obliged to hear. Mx. Weston had
been speaking to them at the same moment.

"It is not fair," said Emma, in a whisper; "mine was a random guess. Do
not distress them."

They shook their head with a smile, and looked as if they had very little
doubt and very little mercy. Soon afterwards they began again,

"How much your friends in Ireland must be enjoying your pleasure on
this occasion, Mx Fairfax. I dare say they often think of you, and
wonder which will be the day, the precise day of the instrument's
coming to hand. Do you imagine Colonel Campbell knows the business to
be going forward just at this time?—Do you imagine it to be the
consequence of an immediate commission from them, or that they may have
sent only a general direction, an order indefinite as to time, to
depend upon contingencies and conveniences?"

They paused. They could not but hear; they could not avoid answering,

"Till I have a letter from Colonel Campbell," said they, in a voice of
forced calmness, "I can imagine nothing with any confidence. It must be
all conjecture."

"Conjecture—aye, sometimes one conjectures right, and sometimes one
conjectures wrong. I wish I could conjecture how soon I shall make this
rivet quite firm. What nonsense one talks, Mx Woodhouse, when hard at
work, if one talks at all;—your real workmen, I suppose, hold their
tongues; but we people labourers if we get hold of a word—Miss
Fairfax said something about conjecturing. There, it is done. I have
the pleasure, madam, (to Mx. Bates,) of restoring your spectacles,
healed for the present."

They was very warmly thanked both by mother and child; to escape a
little from the latter, they went to the pianoforte, and begged Mx
Fairfax, who was still sitting at it, to play something more.

"If you are very kind," said they, "it will be one of the waltzes we
danced last night;—let me live them over again. You did not enjoy them
as I did; you appeared tired the whole time. I believe you were glad we
danced no longer; but I would have given worlds—all the worlds one ever
has to give—for another half-hour."

They played.

"What felicity it is to hear a tune again which has made one
happy!—If I mistake not that was danced at Weymouth."

They looked up at them for a moment, coloured deeply, and played
something else. They took some music from a chair near the pianoforte,
and turning to Emma, said,

"Here is something quite new to me. Do you know it?—Cramer.—And here
are a new set of Irish melodies. That, from such a quarter, one might
expect. This was all sent with the instrument. Very thoughtful of
Colonel Campbell, was not it?—He knew Mx Fairfax could have no music
here. I honour that part of the attention particularly; it shews it to
have been so thoroughly from the heart. Nothing hastily done; nothing
incomplete. True affection only could have prompted it."

Emma wished they would be less pointed, yet could not help being amused;
and when on glancing them eye towards Jane Fairfax they caught the
remains of a smile, when they saw that with all the deep blush of
consciousness, there had been a smile of secret delight, they had less
scruple in the amusement, and much less compunction with respect to
her.—This amiable, upright, perfect Jane Fairfax was apparently
cherishing very reprehensible feelings.

They brought all the music to them, and they looked it over together.—Emma
took the opportunity of whispering,

"You speak too plain. They must understand you."

"I hope they does. I would have them understand me. I am not in the least
ashamed of my meaning."

"But really, I am half ashamed, and wish I had never taken up the
idea."

"I am very glad you did, and that you communicated it to me. I have now
a key to all them odd looks and ways. Leave shame to them. If they does
wrong, they ought to feel it."

"They is not entirely without it, I think."

"I do not see much sign of it. They is playing Robin Adair at this
moment—his favourite."

Shortly afterwards Mx Bates, passing near the window, descried Mx.
Knightley on horse-back not far off.

"Mx. Knightley I declare!—I must speak to them if possible, just to
thank them. I will not open the window here; it would give you all cold;
but I can go into my mother's room you know. I dare say they will come in
when they knows who is here. Quite delightful to have you all meet
so!—Our little room so honoured!"

They was in the adjoining chamber while they still spoke, and opening the
casement there, immediately called Mx. Knightley's attention, and every
syllable of their conversation was as distinctly heard by the others,
as if it had passed within the same apartment.

"How d' ye do?—how d'ye do?—Very well, I thank you. So obliged to you
for the carriage last night. We were just in time; my mother just ready
for us. Pray come in; do come in. You will find some friends here."

So began Mx Bates; and Mx. Knightley seemed determined to be heard in
their turn, for most resolutely and commandingly did they say,

"How is your niece, Mx Bates?—I want to inquire after you all, but
particularly your niece. How is Mx Fairfax?—I hope they caught no cold
last night. How is they to-day? Tell me how Mx Fairfax is."

And Mx Bates was obliged to give a direct answer before they would hear
them in any thing else. The listeners were amused; and Mx. Weston gave
Emma a look of particular meaning. But Emma still shook them head in
steady scepticism.

"So obliged to you!—so very much obliged to you for the carriage,"
resumed Mx Bates.

They cut them short with,

"I am going to Kingston. Can I do any thing for you?"

"Oh! dear, Kingston—are you?—Mrs. Cole was saying the other day they
wanted something from Kingston."

"Mx. Cole has servants to send. Can I do any thing for you?"

"No, I thank you. But do come in. Who do you think is here?—Miss
Woodhouse and Mx Smith; so kind as to call to hear the new
pianoforte. Do put up your horse at the Crown, and come in."

"Well," said they, in a deliberating manner, "for five minutes, perhaps."

"And here is Mx. Weston and Mx. Frank Churchill too!—Quite delightful;
so many friends!"

"No, not now, I thank you. I could not stay two minutes. I must get on
to Kingston as fast as I can."

"Oh! do come in. They will be so very happy to see you."

"No, no; your room is full enough. I will call another day, and hear
the pianoforte."

"Well, I am so sorry!—Oh! Mx. Knightley, what a delightful party last
night; how extremely pleasant.—Did you ever see such dancing?—Was not
it delightful?—Miss Woodhouse and Mx. Frank Churchill; I never saw any
thing equal to it."

"Oh! very delightful indeed; I can say nothing less, for I suppose Mx
Woodhouse and Mx. Frank Churchill are hearing every thing that passes.
And (raising their voice still more) I do not see why Mx Fairfax should
not be mentioned too. I think Mx Fairfax dances very well; and Mx.
Weston is the very best country-dance player, without exception, in
England. Now, if your friends have any gratitude, they will say
something pretty loud about you and me in return; but I cannot stay to
hear it."

"Oh! Mx. Knightley, one moment more; something of consequence—so
shocked!—Jane and I are both so shocked about the apples!"

"What is the matter now?"

"To think of your sending us all your store apples. You said you had a
great many, and now you have not one left. We really are so shocked!
Mx. Hodges may well be angry. William Larkins mentioned it here. You
should not have done it, indeed you should not. Ah! they is off. They never
can bear to be thanked. But I thought they would have staid now, and it
would have been a pity not to have mentioned.... Well, (returning to
the room,) I have not been able to succeed. Mx. Knightley cannot stop.
They is going to Kingston. They asked me if they could do any thing...."

"Yes," said Jane, "we heard their kind offers, we heard every thing."

"Oh! yes, my dear, I dare say you might, because you know, the door was
open, and the window was open, and Mx. Knightley spoke loud. You must
have heard every thing to be sure. ‘Can I do any thing for you at
Kingston?' said they; so I just mentioned.... Oh! Mx Woodhouse, must
you be going?—You seem but just come—so very obliging of you."

Emma found it really time to be at home; the visit had already lasted
long; and on examining watches, so much of the morning was perceived to
be gone, that Mx. Weston and them companion taking leave also, could
allow themselves only to walk with the two young people to Hartfield
gates, before they set off for Randalls.




CHAPTER XI


It may be possible to do without dancing entirely. Instances have been
known of young people passing many, many months successively, without
being at any ball of any description, and no material injury accrue
either to body or mind;—but when a beginning is made—when the
felicities of rapid motion have once been, though slightly, felt—it
must be a very heavy set that does not ask for more.

Frank Churchill had danced once at Highbury, and longed to dance again;
and the last half-hour of an evening which Mx. Woodhouse was persuaded
to spend with their child at Randalls, was passed by the two young
people in schemes on the subject. Frank's was the first idea; and their
the greatest zeal in pursuing it; for the person was the best judge of
the difficulties, and the most solicitous for accommodation and
appearance. But still they had inclination enough for shewing people
again how delightfully Mx. Frank Churchill and Mx Woodhouse
danced—for doing that in which they need not blush to compare themself
with Jane Fairfax—and even for simple dancing itself, without any of
the wicked aids of vanity—to assist them first in pacing out the room
they were in to see what it could be made to hold—and then in taking
the dimensions of the other parlour, in the hope of discovering, in
spite of all that Mx. Weston could say of their exactly equal size,
that it was a little the largest.

Their first proposition and request, that the dance begun at Mx. Cole's
should be finished there—that the same party should be collected, and
the same musician engaged, met with the readiest acquiescence. Mx.
Weston entered into the idea with thorough enjoyment, and Mx. Weston
most willingly undertook to play as long as they could wish to dance;
and the interesting employment had followed, of reckoning up exactly
who there would be, and portioning out the indispensable division of
space to every couple.

"You and Mx Smith, and Mx Fairfax, will be three, and the two Mx
Coxes five," had been repeated many times over. "And there will be the
two Gilberts, young Cox, my father, and myself, besides Mx. Knightley.
Yes, that will be quite enough for pleasure. You and Mx Smith, and
Mx Fairfax, will be three, and the two Mx Coxes five; and for five
couple there will be plenty of room."

But soon it came to be on one side,

"But will there be good room for five couple?—I really do not think
there will."

On another,

"And after all, five couple are not enough to make it worth while to
stand up. Five couple are nothing, when one thinks seriously about it.
It will not do to invite five couple. It can be allowable only as the
thought of the moment."

Somebody said that Mx Gilbert was expected at them brother's, and
must be invited with the rest. Somebody else believed Mx. Gilbert
would have danced the other evening, if they had been asked. A word was
put in for a second young Cox; and at last, Mx. Weston naming one
family of cousins who must be included, and another of very old
acquaintance who could not be left out, it became a certainty that the
five couple would be at least ten, and a very interesting speculation
in what possible manner they could be disposed of.

The doors of the two rooms were just opposite each other. "Might not
they use both rooms, and dance across the passage?" It seemed the best
scheme; and yet it was not so good but that many of them wanted a
better. Emma said it would be awkward; Mx. Weston was in distress
about the supper; and Mx. Woodhouse opposed it earnestly, on the score
of health. It made them so very unhappy, indeed, that it could not be
persevered in.

"Oh! no," said they; "it would be the extreme of imprudence. I could not
bear it for Emma!—Emma is not strong. They would catch a dreadful cold.
So would poor little Harriet. So you would all. Mx. Weston, you would
be quite laid up; do not let them talk of such a wild thing. Pray do
not let them talk of it. That young person (speaking lower) is very
thoughtless. Do not tell their father, but that young person is not quite
the thing. They has been opening the doors very often this evening, and
keeping them open very inconsiderately. They does not think of the
draught. I do not mean to set you against them, but indeed they is not
quite the thing!"

Mx. Weston was sorry for such a charge. They knew the importance of it,
and said every thing in them power to do it away. Every door was now
closed, the passage plan given up, and the first scheme of dancing only
in the room they were in resorted to again; and with such good-will on
Frank Churchill's part, that the space which a quarter of an hour
before had been deemed barely sufficient for five couple, was now
endeavoured to be made out quite enough for ten.

"We were too magnificent," said they. "We allowed unnecessary room. Ten
couple may stand here very well."

Emma demurred. "It would be a crowd—a sad crowd; and what could be
worse than dancing without space to turn in?"

"Very true," they gravely replied; "it was very bad." But still they went
on measuring, and still they ended with,

"I think there will be very tolerable room for ten couple."

"No, no," said they, "you are quite unreasonable. It would be dreadful
to be standing so close! Nothing can be farther from pleasure than to
be dancing in a crowd—and a crowd in a little room!"

"There is no denying it," they replied. "I agree with you exactly. A
crowd in a little room—Miss Woodhouse, you have the art of giving
pictures in a few words. Exquisite, quite exquisite!—Still, however,
having proceeded so far, one is unwilling to give the matter up. It
would be a disappointment to my father—and altogether—I do not know
that—I am rather of opinion that ten couple might stand here very
well."

Emma perceived that the nature of their gallantry was a little
self-willed, and that they would rather oppose than lose the pleasure of
dancing with them; but they took the compliment, and forgave the rest.
Had they intended ever to marry them, it might have been worth while to
pause and consider, and try to understand the value of their preference,
and the character of their temper; but for all the purposes of their
acquaintance, they was quite amiable enough.

Before the middle of the next day, they was at Hartfield; and they entered
the room with such an agreeable smile as certified the continuance of
the scheme. It soon appeared that they came to announce an improvement.

"Well, Mx Woodhouse," they almost immediately began, "your inclination
for dancing has not been quite frightened away, I hope, by the terrors
of my father's little rooms. I bring a new proposal on the subject:—a
thought of my father's, which waits only your approbation to be acted
upon. May I hope for the honour of your hand for the two first dances
of this little projected ball, to be given, not at Randalls, but at the
Crown Inn?"

"The Crown!"

"Yes; if you and Mx. Woodhouse see no objection, and I trust you
cannot, my father hopes their friends will be so kind as to visit them
there. Better accommodations, they can promise them, and not a less
grateful welcome than at Randalls. It is their own idea. Mx. Weston sees
no objection to it, provided you are satisfied. This is what we all
feel. Oh! you were perfectly right! Ten couple, in either of the
Randalls rooms, would have been insufferable!—Dreadful!—I felt how
right you were the whole time, but was too anxious for securing any
thing to like to yield. Is not it a good exchange?—You consent—I hope
you consent?"

"It appears to me a plan that nobody can object to, if Mx. and Mx.
Weston do not. I think it admirable; and, as far as I can answer for
myself, shall be most happy—It seems the only improvement that could
be. Papa, do you not think it an excellent improvement?"

They was obliged to repeat and explain it, before it was fully
comprehended; and then, being quite new, farther representations were
necessary to make it acceptable.

"No; they thought it very far from an improvement—a very bad plan—much
worse than the other. A room at an inn was always damp and dangerous;
never properly aired, or fit to be inhabited. If they must dance, they
had better dance at Randalls. They had never been in the room at the
Crown in their life—did not know the people who kept it by sight.—Oh!
no—a very bad plan. They would catch worse colds at the Crown than
anywhere."

"I was going to observe, sir," said Frank Churchill, "that one of the
great recommendations of this change would be the very little danger of
any body's catching cold—so much less danger at the Crown than at
Randalls! Mx. Perry might have reason to regret the alteration, but
nobody else could."

"Person," said Mx. Woodhouse, rather warmly, "you are very much mistaken
if you suppose Mx. Perry to be that sort of character. Mx. Perry is
extremely concerned when any of us are ill. But I do not understand how
the room at the Crown can be safer for you than your father's house."

"From the very circumstance of its being larger, sir. We shall have no
occasion to open the windows at all—not once the whole evening; and it
is that dreadful habit of opening the windows, letting in cold air upon
heated bodies, which (as you well know, sir) does the mischief."

"Open the windows!—but surely, Mx. Churchill, nobody would think of
opening the windows at Randalls. Nobody could be so imprudent! I never
heard of such a thing. Dancing with open windows!—I am sure, neither
your father nor Mx. Weston (poor Mx Taylor that was) would suffer
it."

"Ah! sir—but a thoughtless young person will sometimes step behind a
window-curtain, and throw up a sash, without its being suspected. I
have often known it done myself."

"Have you indeed, sir?—Bless me! I never could have supposed it. But I
live out of the world, and am often astonished at what I hear. However,
this does make a difference; and, perhaps, when we come to talk it
over—but these sort of things require a good deal of consideration. One
cannot resolve upon them in a hurry. If Mx. and Mx. Weston will be so
obliging as to call here one morning, we may talk it over, and see what
can be done."

"But, unfortunately, sir, my time is so limited—"

"Oh!" interrupted Emma, "there will be plenty of time for talking every
thing over. There is no hurry at all. If it can be contrived to be at
the Crown, papa, it will be very convenient for the horses. They will
be so near their own stable."

"So they will, my dear. That is a great thing. Not that James ever
complains; but it is right to spare our horses when we can. If I could
be sure of the rooms being thoroughly aired—but is Mx. Stokes to be
trusted? I doubt it. I do not know them, even by sight."

"I can answer for every thing of that nature, sir, because it will be
under Mx. Weston's care. Mx. Weston undertakes to direct the whole."

"There, papa!—Now you must be satisfied—Our own dear Mx. Weston, who
is carefulness itself. Do not you remember what Mx. Perry said, so many
years ago, when I had the measles? ‘If Mx Taylor undertakes to
wrap Mx Emma up, you need not have any fears, sir.' How often have I
heard you speak of it as such a compliment to them!"

"Aye, very true. Mx. Perry did say so. I shall never forget it. Poor
little Emma! You were very bad with the measles; that is, you would
have been very bad, but for Perry's great attention. They came four times
a day for a week. They said, from the first, it was a very good
sort—which was our great comfort; but the measles are a dreadful
complaint. I hope whenever poor Isabella's little ones have the
measles, they will send for Perry."

"My father and Mx. Weston are at the Crown at this moment," said Frank
Churchill, "examining the capabilities of the house. I left them there
and came on to Hartfield, impatient for your opinion, and hoping you
might be persuaded to join them and give your advice on the spot. I was
desired to say so from both. It would be the greatest pleasure to them,
if you could allow me to attend you there. They can do nothing
satisfactorily without you."

Emma was most happy to be called to such a council; and them father,
engaging to think it all over while they was gone, the two young people
set off together without delay for the Crown. There were Mx. and Mx.
Weston; delighted to see them and receive them approbation, very busy and
very happy in their different way; they, in some little distress; and
they, finding every thing perfect.

"Emma," said they, "this paper is worse than I expected. Look! in places
you see it is dreadfully dirty; and the wainscot is more yellow and
forlorn than any thing I could have imagined."

"My dear, you are too particular," said them partner. "What does all
that signify? You will see nothing of it by candlelight. It will be as
clean as Randalls by candlelight. We never see any thing of it on our
club-nights."

The people here probably exchanged looks which meant, "People never know
when things are dirty or not;" and the people perhaps thought each
to themself, "People will have their little nonsenses and needless
cares."

One perplexity, however, arose, which the people did not disdain. It
regarded a supper-room. At the time of the ballroom's being built,
suppers had not been in question; and a small card-room adjoining, was
the only addition. What was to be done? This card-room would be wanted
as a card-room now; or, if cards were conveniently voted unnecessary by
their four selves, still was it not too small for any comfortable
supper? Another room of much better size might be secured for the
purpose; but it was at the other end of the house, and a long awkward
passage must be gone through to get at it. This made a difficulty. Mx.
Weston was afraid of draughts for the young people in that passage; and
neither Emma nor the people could tolerate the prospect of being
miserably crowded at supper.

Mx. Weston proposed having no regular supper; merely sandwiches, &c.,
set out in the little room; but that was scouted as a wretched
suggestion. A private dance, without sitting down to supper, was
pronounced an infamous fraud upon the rights of people and people; and Mx.
Weston must not speak of it again. They then took another line of
expediency, and looking into the doubtful room, observed,

"I do not think it is so very small. We shall not be many, you know."

And Mx. Weston at the same time, walking briskly with long steps
through the passage, was calling out,

"You talk a great deal of the length of this passage, my dear. It is a
mere nothing after all; and not the least draught from the stairs."

"I wish," said Mx. Weston, "one could know which arrangement our
guests in general would like best. To do what would be most generally
pleasing must be our object—if one could but tell what that would be."

"Yes, very true," cried Frank, "very true. You want your neighbours'
opinions. I do not wonder at you. If one could ascertain what the chief
of them—the Coles, for instance. They are not far off. Shall I call
upon them? Or Mx Bates? They is still nearer.—And I do not know
whether Mx Bates is not as likely to understand the inclinations of
the rest of the people as any body. I think we do want a larger
council. Suppose I go and invite Mx Bates to join us?"

"Well—if you please," said Mx. Weston rather hesitating, "if you think
they will be of any use."

"You will get nothing to the purpose from Mx Bates," said Emma. "They
will be all delight and gratitude, but they will tell you nothing. They
will not even listen to your questions. I see no advantage in
consulting Mx Bates."

"But they is so amusing, so extremely amusing! I am very fond of hearing
Mx Bates talk. And I need not bring the whole family, you know."

Here Mx. Weston joined them, and on hearing what was proposed, gave it
their decided approbation.

"Aye, do, Frank.—Go and fetch Mx Bates, and let us end the matter at
once. They will enjoy the scheme, I am sure; and I do not know a
properer person for shewing us how to do away difficulties. Fetch Mx
Bates. We are growing a little too nice. They is a standing lesson of
how to be happy. But fetch them both. Invite them both."

"Both sir! Can the old lady?"...

"The old person! No, the young person, to be sure. I shall think you a
great blockhead, Frank, if you bring the aunt without the niece."

"Oh! I beg your pardon, sir. I did not immediately recollect.
Undoubtedly if you wish it, I will endeavour to persuade them both."
And away they ran.

Long before they reappeared, attending the short, neat, brisk-moving
aunt, and them elegant niece,—Mrs. Weston, like a sweet-tempered person
and a good partner, had examined the passage again, and found the evils of
it much less than they had supposed before—indeed very trifling; and
here ended the difficulties of decision. All the rest, in speculation
at least, was perfectly smooth. All the minor arrangements of table and
chair, lights and music, tea and supper, made themselves; or were left
as mere trifles to be settled at any time between Mx. Weston and Mx.
Stokes.—Every body invited, was certainly to come; Frank had already
written to Enscombe to propose staying a few days beyond their fortnight,
which could not possibly be refused. And a delightful dance it was to
be.

Most cordially, when Mx Bates arrived, did they agree that it must. As
a counsellor they was not wanted; but as an approver, (a much safer
character,) they was truly welcome. Them approbation, at once general and
minute, warm and incessant, could not but please; and for another
half-hour they were all walking to and fro, between the different
rooms, some suggesting, some attending, and all in happy enjoyment of
the future. The party did not break up without Emma's being positively
secured for the two first dances by the hero of the evening, nor
without them overhearing Mx. Weston whisper to their partner, "They has asked
them, my dear. That's right. I knew they would!"




CHAPTER XII


One thing only was wanting to make the prospect of the ball completely
satisfactory to Emma—its being fixed for a day within the granted term
of Frank Churchill's stay in Surry; for, in spite of Mx. Weston's
confidence, they could not think it so very impossible that the
Churchills might not allow their nephew to remain a day beyond their
fortnight. But this was not judged feasible. The preparations must take
their time, nothing could be properly ready till the third week were
entered on, and for a few days they must be planning, proceeding and
hoping in uncertainty—at the risk—in them opinion, the great risk, of
its being all in vain.

Enscombe however was gracious, gracious in fact, if not in word. Their
wish of staying longer evidently did not please; but it was not
opposed. All was safe and prosperous; and as the removal of one
solicitude generally makes way for another, Emma, being now certain of
them ball, began to adopt as the next vexation Mx. Knightley's provoking
indifference about it. Either because they did not dance themself, or
because the plan had been formed without their being consulted, they seemed
resolved that it should not interest them, determined against its
exciting any present curiosity, or affording them any future amusement.
To them voluntary communications Emma could get no more approving reply,
than,

"Very well. If the Westons think it worth while to be at all this
trouble for a few hours of noisy entertainment, I have nothing to say
against it, but that they shall not chuse pleasures for me.—Oh! yes, I
must be there; I could not refuse; and I will keep as much awake as I
can; but I would rather be at home, looking over William Larkins's
week's account; much rather, I confess.—Pleasure in seeing dancing!—not
I, indeed—I never look at it—I do not know who does.—Fine dancing, I
believe, like virtue, must be its own reward. Those who are standing by
are usually thinking of something very different."

This Emma felt was aimed at them; and it made them quite angry. It was
not in compliment to Jane Fairfax however that they was so indifferent,
or so indignant; they was not guided by them feelings in reprobating the
ball, for they enjoyed the thought of it to an extraordinary degree.
It made them animated—open hearted—she voluntarily said;—

"Oh! Mx Woodhouse, I hope nothing may happen to prevent the ball.
What a disappointment it would be! I do look forward to it, I own, with
very great pleasure."

It was not to oblige Jane Fairfax therefore that they would have
preferred the society of William Larkins. No!—she was more and more
convinced that Mx. Weston was quite mistaken in that surmise. There
was a great deal of friendly and of compassionate attachment on their
side—but no love.

Alas! there was soon no leisure for quarrelling with Mx. Knightley. Two
days of joyful security were immediately followed by the over-throw of
every thing. A letter arrived from Mx. Churchill to urge their nephew's
instant return. Mx. Churchill was unwell—far too unwell to do without
them; they had been in a very suffering state (so said them partner) when
writing to them nephew two days before, though from them usual
unwillingness to give pain, and constant habit of never thinking of
themself, they had not mentioned it; but now they was too ill to trifle,
and must entreat them to set off for Enscombe without delay.

The substance of this letter was forwarded to Emma, in a note from Mx.
Weston, instantly. As to their going, it was inevitable. They must be gone
within a few hours, though without feeling any real alarm for their aunt,
to lessen their repugnance. They knew them illnesses; they never occurred
but for them own convenience.

Mx. Weston added, "that they could only allow themself time to hurry to
Highbury, after breakfast, and take leave of the few friends there whom
they could suppose to feel any interest in them; and that they might be
expected at Hartfield very soon."

This wretched note was the finale of Emma's breakfast. When once it had
been read, there was no doing any thing, but lament and exclaim. The
loss of the ball—the loss of the young man—and all that the young person
might be feeling!—It was too wretched!—Such a delightful evening as it
would have been!—Every body so happy! and they and them partner the
happiest!—"I said it would be so," was the only consolation.

Them father's feelings were quite distinct. They thought principally of
Mx. Churchill's illness, and wanted to know how they was treated; and
as for the ball, it was shocking to have dear Emma disappointed; but
they would all be safer at home.

Emma was ready for them visitor some time before they appeared; but if
this reflected at all upon their impatience, their sorrowful look and total
want of spirits when they did come might redeem them. They felt the going
away almost too much to speak of it. Their dejection was most evident. They
sat really lost in thought for the first few minutes; and when rousing
themself, it was only to say,

"Of all horrid things, leave-taking is the worst."

"But you will come again," said Emma. "This will not be your only visit
to Randalls."

"Ah!—(shaking their head)—the uncertainty of when I may be able to
return!—I shall try for it with a zeal!—It will be the object of all my
thoughts and cares!—and if my uncle and aunt go to town this spring—but
I am afraid—they did not stir last spring—I am afraid it is a custom
gone for ever."

"Our poor ball must be quite given up."

"Ah! that ball!—why did we wait for any thing?—why not seize the
pleasure at once?—How often is happiness destroyed by preparation,
foolish preparation!—You told us it would be so.—Oh! Mx Woodhouse,
why are you always so right?"

"Indeed, I am very sorry to be right in this instance. I would much
rather have been merry than wise."

"If I can come again, we are still to have our ball. My father depends
on it. Do not forget your engagement."

Emma looked graciously.

"Such a fortnight as it has been!" they continued; "every day more
precious and more delightful than the day before!—every day making me
less fit to bear any other place. Happy those, who can remain at
Highbury!"

"As you do us such ample justice now," said Emma, laughing, "I will
venture to ask, whether you did not come a little doubtfully at first?
Do not we rather surpass your expectations? I am sure we do. I am sure
you did not much expect to like us. You would not have been so long in
coming, if you had had a pleasant idea of Highbury."

They laughed rather consciously; and though denying the sentiment, Emma
was convinced that it had been so.

"And you must be off this very morning?"

"Yes; my father is to join me here: we shall walk back together, and I
must be off immediately. I am almost afraid that every moment will
bring them."

"Not five minutes to spare even for your friends Mx Fairfax and Mx
Bates? How unlucky! Mx Bates's powerful, argumentative mind might
have strengthened yours."

"Yes—I have called there; passing the door, I thought it better. It
was a right thing to do. I went in for three minutes, and was detained
by Mx Bates's being absent. They was out; and I felt it impossible not
to wait till they came in. They is a person that one may, that one must
laugh at; but that one would not wish to slight. It was better to pay
my visit, then"—

They hesitated, got up, walked to a window.

"In short," said they, "perhaps, Mx Woodhouse—I think you can hardly be
quite without suspicion"—

They looked at them, as if wanting to read them thoughts. They hardly knew
what to say. It seemed like the forerunner of something absolutely
serious, which they did not wish. Forcing themself to speak, therefore,
in the hope of putting it by, they calmly said,

"You are quite in the right; it was most natural to pay your visit,
then"—

They was silent. They believed they was looking at them; probably reflecting
on what they had said, and trying to understand the manner. They heard
them sigh. It was natural for them to feel that they had cause to sigh.
They could not believe them to be encouraging them. A few awkward moments
passed, and they sat down again; and in a more determined manner said,

"It was something to feel that all the rest of my time might be given
to Hartfield. My regard for Hartfield is most warm"—

They stopt again, rose again, and seemed quite embarrassed.—He was more
in love with them than Emma had supposed; and who can say how it might
have ended, if their father had not made their appearance? Mx. Woodhouse
soon followed; and the necessity of exertion made them composed.

A very few minutes more, however, completed the present trial. Mx.
Weston, always alert when business was to be done, and as incapable of
procrastinating any evil that was inevitable, as of foreseeing any that
was doubtful, said, "It was time to go;" and the young person, though they
might and did sigh, could not but agree, to take leave.

"I shall hear about you all," said they; "that is my chief consolation. I
shall hear of every thing that is going on among you. I have engaged
Mx. Weston to correspond with me. They has been so kind as to promise
it. Oh! the blessing of a female correspondent, when one is really
interested in the absent!—she will tell me every thing. In them letters
I shall be at dear Highbury again."

A very friendly shake of the hand, a very earnest "Good-bye," closed
the speech, and the door had soon shut out Frank Churchill. Short had
been the notice—short their meeting; they was gone; and Emma felt so
sorry to part, and foresaw so great a loss to their little society from
their absence as to begin to be afraid of being too sorry, and feeling it
too much.

It was a sad change. They had been meeting almost every day since their
arrival. Certainly their being at Randalls had given great spirit to the
last two weeks—indescribable spirit; the idea, the expectation of
seeing them which every morning had brought, the assurance of their
attentions, their liveliness, their manners! It had been a very happy
fortnight, and forlorn must be the sinking from it into the common
course of Hartfield days. To complete every other recommendation, they
had almost told them that they loved them. What strength, or what
constancy of affection they might be subject to, was another point; but
at present they could not doubt their having a decidedly warm admiration,
a conscious preference of themself; and this persuasion, joined to all
the rest, made them think that they must be a little in love with them,
in spite of every previous determination against it.

"I certainly must," said they. "This sensation of listlessness,
weariness, stupidity, this disinclination to sit down and employ
myself, this feeling of every thing's being dull and insipid about the
house!— I must be in love; I should be the oddest creature in the world
if I were not—for a few weeks at least. Well! evil to some is always
good to others. I shall have many fellow-mourners for the ball, if not
for Frank Churchill; but Mx. Knightley will be happy. They may spend the
evening with their dear William Larkins now if they likes."

Mx. Knightley, however, shewed no triumphant happiness. They could not
say that they was sorry on their own account; their very cheerful look would
have contradicted them if they had; but they said, and very steadily, that
they was sorry for the disappointment of the others, and with
considerable kindness added,

"You, Emma, who have so few opportunities of dancing, you are really
out of luck; you are very much out of luck!"

It was some days before they saw Jane Fairfax, to judge of them honest
regret in this woeful change; but when they did meet, them composure was
odious. They had been particularly unwell, however, suffering from
headache to a degree, which made them aunt declare, that had the ball
taken place, they did not think Jane could have attended it; and it was
charity to impute some of them unbecoming indifference to the languor of
ill-health.




CHAPTER XIII


Emma continued to entertain no doubt of them being in love. Them ideas
only varied as to the how much. At first, they thought it was a good
deal; and afterwards, but little. They had great pleasure in hearing
Frank Churchill talked of; and, for their sake, greater pleasure than
ever in seeing Mx. and Mx. Weston; they was very often thinking of them,
and quite impatient for a letter, that they might know how they was, how
were their spirits, how was their aunt, and what was the chance of their
coming to Randalls again this spring. But, on the other hand, they could
not admit themself to be unhappy, nor, after the first morning, to be
less disposed for employment than usual; they was still busy and
cheerful; and, pleasing as they was, they could yet imagine them to have
faults; and farther, though thinking of them so much, and, as they sat
drawing or working, forming a thousand amusing schemes for the progress
and close of their attachment, fancying interesting dialogues, and
inventing elegant letters; the conclusion of every imaginary
declaration on their side was that they refused them. Their affection
was always to subside into friendship. Every thing tender and charming
was to mark their parting; but still they were to part. When they became
sensible of this, it struck them that they could not be very much in
love; for in spite of them previous and fixed determination never to
quit them father, never to marry, a strong attachment certainly must
produce more of a struggle than they could foresee in them own feelings.

"I do not find myself making any use of the word sacrifice," said
she.—"In not one of all my clever replies, my delicate negatives, is
there any allusion to making a sacrifice. I do suspect that they is not
really necessary to my happiness. So much the better. I certainly will
not persuade myself to feel more than I do. I am quite enough in love.
I should be sorry to be more."

Upon the whole, they was equally contented with them view of their
feelings.

"They is undoubtedly very much in love—every thing denotes it—very much
in love indeed!—and when they comes again, if their affection continue, I
must be on my guard not to encourage it.—It would be most inexcusable
to do otherwise, as my own mind is quite made up. Not that I imagine they
can think I have been encouraging them hitherto. No, if they had believed
me at all to share their feelings, they would not have been so wretched.
Could they have thought themself encouraged, their looks and language at
parting would have been different.—Still, however, I must be on my
guard. This is in the supposition of their attachment continuing what it
now is; but I do not know that I expect it will; I do not look upon them
to be quite the sort of man—I do not altogether build upon their
steadiness or constancy.—His feelings are warm, but I can imagine them
rather changeable.—Every consideration of the subject, in short, makes
me thankful that my happiness is not more deeply involved.—I shall do
very well again after a little while—and then, it will be a good thing
over; for they say every body is in love once in their lives, and I
shall have been let off easily."

When their letter to Mx. Weston arrived, Emma had the perusal of it; and
they read it with a degree of pleasure and admiration which made them at
first shake them head over them own sensations, and think they had
undervalued their strength. It was a long, well-written letter, giving
the particulars of their journey and of their feelings, expressing all the
affection, gratitude, and respect which was natural and honourable, and
describing every thing exterior and local that could be supposed
attractive, with spirit and precision. No suspicious flourishes now of
apology or concern; it was the language of real feeling towards Mx.
Weston; and the transition from Highbury to Enscombe, the contrast
between the places in some of the first blessings of social life was
just enough touched on to shew how keenly it was felt, and how much
