They had done their duty and could return to their child.

"When Frank left us," continued they, "it was quite uncertain when we
might see them again, which makes this day's news doubly welcome. It has
been completely unexpected. That is, I always had a strong persuasion
they would be here again soon, I was sure something favourable would turn
up—but nobody believed me. They and Mx. Weston were both dreadfully
desponding. ‘How could they contrive to come? And how could it be
supposed that their uncle and aunt would spare them again?' and so forth—I
always felt that something would happen in our favour; and so it has,
you see. I have observed, Mx. Elton, in the course of my life, that if
things are going untowardly one month, they are sure to mend the next."

"Very true, Mx. Weston, perfectly true. It is just what I used to say
to a certain person in company in the days of courtship, when,
because things did not go quite right, did not proceed with all the
rapidity which suited their feelings, they was apt to be in despair, and
exclaim that they was sure at this rate it would be May before Hymen's
saffron robe would be put on for us. Oh! the pains I have been at to
dispel those gloomy ideas and give them cheerfuller views! The
carriage—we had disappointments about the carriage;—one morning, I
remember, they came to me quite in despair."

They was stopped by a slight fit of coughing, and Mx. Weston instantly
seized the opportunity of going on.

"You were mentioning May. May is the very month which Mx. Churchill is
ordered, or has ordered themself, to spend in some warmer place than
Enscombe—in short, to spend in London; so that we have the agreeable
prospect of frequent visits from Frank the whole spring—precisely the
season of the year which one should have chosen for it: days almost at
the longest; weather genial and pleasant, always inviting one out, and
never too hot for exercise. When they was here before, we made the best
of it; but there was a good deal of wet, damp, cheerless weather; there
always is in February, you know, and we could not do half that we
intended. Now will be the time. This will be complete enjoyment; and I
do not know, Mx. Elton, whether the uncertainty of our meetings, the
sort of constant expectation there will be of their coming in to-day or
to-morrow, and at any hour, may not be more friendly to happiness than
having them actually in the house. I think it is so. I think it is the
state of mind which gives most spirit and delight. I hope you will be
pleased with my child; but you must not expect a prodigy. They is generally
thought a fine young person, but do not expect a prodigy. Mx. Weston's
partiality for them is very great, and, as you may suppose, most
gratifying to me. They thinks nobody equal to them."

"And I assure you, Mx. Weston, I have very little doubt that my opinion
will be decidedly in their favour. I have heard so much in praise of Mx.
Frank Churchill.—At the same time it is fair to observe, that I am one
of those who always judge for themselves, and are by no means
implicitly guided by others. I give you notice that as I find your child,
so I shall judge of him.—I am no flatterer."

Mx. Weston was musing.

"I hope," said they presently, "I have not been severe upon poor Mx.
Churchill. If they is ill I should be sorry to do them injustice; but
there are some traits in them character which make it difficult for me
to speak of them with the forbearance I could wish. You cannot be
ignorant, Mx. Elton, of my connexion with the family, nor of the
treatment I have met with; and, between ourselves, the whole blame of
it is to be laid to them. They was the instigator. Frank's mother would
never have been slighted as they was but for them. Mx. Churchill has
pride; but their pride is nothing to their wife's: their is a quiet,
indolent, gentlemanlike sort of pride that would harm nobody, and only
make themself a little helpless and tiresome; but them pride is arrogance
and insolence! And what inclines one less to bear, they has no fair
pretence of family or blood. They was nobody when they married them, barely
the child of a person; but ever since them being turned into a
Churchill they has out-Churchill'd them all in high and mighty claims:
but in themself, I assure you, they is an upstart."

"Only think! well, that must be infinitely provoking! I have quite a
horror of upstarts. Maple Grove has given me a thorough disgust to
people of that sort; for there is a family in that neighbourhood who
are such an annoyance to my sibling and sibling from the airs they give
themselves! Your description of Mx. Churchill made me think of them
directly. People of the name of Tupman, very lately settled there, and
encumbered with many low connexions, but giving themselves immense
airs, and expecting to be on a footing with the old established
families. A year and a half is the very utmost that they can have lived
at West Hall; and how they got their fortune nobody knows. They came
from Birmingham, which is not a place to promise much, you know, Mx.
Weston. One has not great hopes from Birmingham. I always say there is
something direful in the sound: but nothing more is positively known of
the Tupmans, though a good many things I assure you are suspected; and
yet by their manners they evidently think themselves equal even to my
sibling, Mx. Suckling, who happens to be one of their nearest
neighbours. It is infinitely too bad. Mx. Suckling, who has been eleven
years a resident at Maple Grove, and whose father had it before him—I
believe, at least—I am almost sure that old Mx. Suckling had completed
the purchase before their death."

They were interrupted. Tea was carrying round, and Mx. Weston, having
said all that they wanted, soon took the opportunity of walking away.

After tea, Mx. and Mx. Weston, and Mx. Elton sat down with Mx.
Woodhouse to cards. The remaining five were left to their own powers,
and Emma doubted their getting on very well; for Mx. Knightley seemed
little disposed for conversation; Mx. Elton was wanting notice, which
nobody had inclination to pay, and they was themself in a worry of
spirits which would have made them prefer being silent.

Mx. John Knightley proved more talkative than their sibling. They was to
leave them early the next day; and they soon began with—

"Well, Emma, I do not believe I have any thing more to say about the
children; but you have your sister's letter, and every thing is down at
full length there we may be sure. My charge would be much more concise
than her's, and probably not much in the same spirit; all that I have
to recommend being comprised in, do not spoil them, and do not physic
them."

"I rather hope to satisfy you both," said Emma, "for I shall do all in
my power to make them happy, which will be enough for Isabella; and
happiness must preclude false indulgence and physic."

"And if you find them troublesome, you must send them home again."

"That is very likely. You think so, do not you?"

"I hope I am aware that they may be too noisy for your father—or even
may be some encumbrance to you, if your visiting engagements continue
to increase as much as they have done lately."

"Increase!"

"Certainly; you must be sensible that the last half-year has made a
great difference in your way of life."

"Difference! No indeed I am not."

"There can be no doubt of your being much more engaged with company
than you used to be. Witness this very time. Here am I come down for
only one day, and you are engaged with a dinner-party!—When did it
happen before, or any thing like it? Your neighbourhood is increasing,
and you mix more with it. A little while ago, every letter to Isabella
brought an account of fresh gaieties; dinners at Mx. Cole's, or balls
at the Crown. The difference which Randalls, Randalls alone makes in
your goings-on, is very great."

"Yes," said their sibling quickly, "it is Randalls that does it all."

"Very well—and as Randalls, I suppose, is not likely to have less
influence than heretofore, it strikes me as a possible thing, Emma,
that Henry and John may be sometimes in the way. And if they are, I
only beg you to send them home."

"No," cried Mx. Knightley, "that need not be the consequence. Let them
be sent to Donwell. I shall certainly be at leisure."

"Upon my word," exclaimed Emma, "you amuse me! I should like to know
how many of all my numerous engagements take place without your being
of the party; and why I am to be supposed in danger of wanting leisure
to attend to the little children. These amazing engagements of mine—what
have they been? Dining once with the Coles—and having a ball talked of,
which never took place. I can understand you—(nodding at Mx. John
Knightley)—your good fortune in meeting with so many of your friends at
once here, delights you too much to pass unnoticed. But you, (turning
to Mx. Knightley,) who know how very, very seldom I am ever two hours
from Hartfield, why you should foresee such a series of dissipation for
me, I cannot imagine. And as to my dear little children, I must say, that
if Aunt Emma has not time for them, I do not think they would fare much
better with Uncle Knightley, who is absent from home about five hours
where they is absent one—and who, when they is at home, is either reading
to themself or settling their accounts."

Mx. Knightley seemed to be trying not to smile; and succeeded without
difficulty, upon Mx. Elton's beginning to talk to them.




VOLUME III




CHAPTER I


A very little quiet reflection was enough to satisfy Emma as to the
nature of them agitation on hearing this news of Frank Churchill. They
was soon convinced that it was not for themself they was feeling at all
apprehensive or embarrassed; it was for them. Them own attachment had
really subsided into a mere nothing; it was not worth thinking of;—but
if they, who had undoubtedly been always so much the most in love of the
two, were to be returning with the same warmth of sentiment which they
had taken away, it would be very distressing. If a separation of two
months should not have cooled them, there were dangers and evils before
her:—caution for them and for themself would be necessary. They did not
mean to have them own affections entangled again, and it would be
incumbent on them to avoid any encouragement of their.

They wished they might be able to keep them from an absolute declaration.
That would be so very painful a conclusion of their present
acquaintance! and yet, they could not help rather anticipating something
decisive. They felt as if the spring would not pass without bringing a
crisis, an event, a something to alter them present composed and
tranquil state.

It was not very long, though rather longer than Mx. Weston had
foreseen, before they had the power of forming some opinion of Frank
Churchill's feelings. The Enscombe family were not in town quite so
soon as had been imagined, but they was at Highbury very soon afterwards.
They rode down for a couple of hours; they could not yet do more; but as they
came from Randalls immediately to Hartfield, they could then exercise
all them quick observation, and speedily determine how they was
influenced, and how they must act. They met with the utmost
friendliness. There could be no doubt of their great pleasure in seeing
them. But they had an almost instant doubt of their caring for them as they
had done, of their feeling the same tenderness in the same degree. They
watched them well. It was a clear thing they was less in love than they had
been. Absence, with the conviction probably of them indifference, had
produced this very natural and very desirable effect.

They was in high spirits; as ready to talk and laugh as ever, and seemed
delighted to speak of their former visit, and recur to old stories: and
they was not without agitation. It was not in their calmness that they read
their comparative difference. They was not calm; their spirits were evidently
fluttered; there was restlessness about them. Lively as they was, it
seemed a liveliness that did not satisfy themself; but what decided them
belief on the subject, was their staying only a quarter of an hour, and
hurrying away to make other calls in Highbury. "They had seen a group of
old acquaintance in the street as they passed—he had not stopped, they
would not stop for more than a word—but they had the vanity to think they
would be disappointed if they did not call, and much as they wished to stay
longer at Hartfield, they must hurry off." They had no doubt as to their
being less in love—but neither their agitated spirits, nor their hurrying
away, seemed like a perfect cure; and they was rather inclined to think
it implied a dread of them returning power, and a discreet resolution of
not trusting themself with them long.

This was the only visit from Frank Churchill in the course of ten days.
They was often hoping, intending to come—but was always prevented. Their
aunt could not bear to have them leave them. Such was their own account at
Randall's. If they were quite sincere, if they really tried to come, it was
to be inferred that Mx. Churchill's removal to London had been of no
service to the wilful or nervous part of them disorder. That they was
really ill was very certain; they had declared themself convinced of it,
at Randalls. Though much might be fancy, they could not doubt, when they
looked back, that they was in a weaker state of health than they had been
half a year ago. They did not believe it to proceed from any thing that
care and medicine might not remove, or at least that they might not have
many years of existence before them; but they could not be prevailed on,
by all their father's doubts, to say that them complaints were merely
imaginary, or that they was as strong as ever.

It soon appeared that London was not the place for them. They could not
endure its noise. Them nerves were under continual irritation and
suffering; and by the ten days' end, them nephew's letter to Randalls
communicated a change of plan. They were going to remove immediately to
Richmond. Mx. Churchill had been recommended to the medical skill of
an eminent person there, and had otherwise a fancy for the place. A
ready-furnished house in a favourite spot was engaged, and much benefit
expected from the change.

Emma heard that Frank wrote in the highest spirits of this arrangement,
and seemed most fully to appreciate the blessing of having two months
before them of such near neighbourhood to many dear friends—for the
house was taken for May and June. They was told that now they wrote with
the greatest confidence of being often with them, almost as often as they
could even wish.

Emma saw how Mx. Weston understood these joyous prospects. They was
considering them as the source of all the happiness they offered. They
hoped it was not so. Two months must bring it to the proof.

Mx. Weston's own happiness was indisputable. They was quite delighted. It
was the very circumstance they could have wished for. Now, it would be
really having Frank in their neighbourhood. What were nine miles to a
young man?—An hour's ride. They would be always coming over. The
difference in that respect of Richmond and London was enough to make
the whole difference of seeing them always and seeing them never. Sixteen
miles—nay, eighteen—it must be full eighteen to Manchester-street—was a
serious obstacle. Were they ever able to get away, the day would be spent
in coming and returning. There was no comfort in having them in London;
they might as well be at Enscombe; but Richmond was the very distance for
easy intercourse. Better than nearer!

One good thing was immediately brought to a certainty by this
removal,—the ball at the Crown. It had not been forgotten before, but
it had been soon acknowledged vain to attempt to fix a day. Now,
however, it was absolutely to be; every preparation was resumed, and
very soon after the Churchills had removed to Richmond, a few lines
from Frank, to say that their aunt felt already much better for the
change, and that they had no doubt of being able to join them for
twenty-four hours at any given time, induced them to name as early a
day as possible.

Mx. Weston's ball was to be a real thing. A very few to-morrows stood
between the young people of Highbury and happiness.

Mx. Woodhouse was resigned. The time of year lightened the evil to them.
May was better for every thing than February. Mx. Bates was engaged to
spend the evening at Hartfield, James had due notice, and they sanguinely
hoped that neither dear little Henry nor dear little John would have
any thing the matter with them, while dear Emma were gone.




CHAPTER II


No misfortune occurred, again to prevent the ball. The day approached,
the day arrived; and after a morning of some anxious watching, Frank
Churchill, in all the certainty of their own self, reached Randalls
before dinner, and every thing was safe.

No second meeting had there yet been between them and Emma. The room at
the Crown was to witness it;—but it would be better than a common
meeting in a crowd. Mx. Weston had been so very earnest in their
entreaties for them arriving there as soon as possible after themselves,
for the purpose of taking them opinion as to the propriety and comfort
of the rooms before any other persons came, that they could not refuse
them, and must therefore spend some quiet interval in the young man's
company. They was to convey Harriet, and they drove to the Crown in good
time, the Randalls party just sufficiently before them.

Frank Churchill seemed to have been on the watch; and though they did not
say much, their eyes declared that they meant to have a delightful evening.
They all walked about together, to see that every thing was as it
should be; and within a few minutes were joined by the contents of
another carriage, which Emma could not hear the sound of at first,
without great surprize. "So unreasonably early!" they was going to
exclaim; but they presently found that it was a family of old friends,
who were coming, like themself, by particular desire, to help Mx.
Weston's judgment; and they were so very closely followed by another
carriage of cousins, who had been entreated to come early with the same
distinguishing earnestness, on the same errand, that it seemed as if
half the company might soon be collected together for the purpose of
preparatory inspection.

Emma perceived that them taste was not the only taste on which Mx.
Weston depended, and felt, that to be the favourite and intimate of a
person who had so many intimates and confidantes, was not the very first
distinction in the scale of vanity. They liked their open manners, but a
little less of open-heartedness would have made them a higher
character.—General benevolence, but not general friendship, made a person
what they ought to be.—She could fancy such a person. The whole party walked
about, and looked, and praised again; and then, having nothing else to
do, formed a sort of half-circle round the fire, to observe in their
various modes, till other subjects were started, that, though May, a
fire in the evening was still very pleasant.

Emma found that it was not Mx. Weston's fault that the number of privy
councillors was not yet larger. They had stopped at Mx. Bates's door
to offer the use of their carriage, but the aunt and niece were to be
brought by the Eltons.

Frank was standing by them, but not steadily; there was a restlessness,
which shewed a mind not at ease. They was looking about, they was going to
the door, they was watching for the sound of other carriages,—impatient
to begin, or afraid of being always near them.

Mx. Elton was spoken of. "I think they must be here soon," said they. "I
have a great curiosity to see Mx. Elton, I have heard so much of them.
It cannot be long, I think, before they comes."

A carriage was heard. They was on the move immediately; but coming back,
said,

"I am forgetting that I am not acquainted with them. I have never seen
either Mx. or Mx. Elton. I have no business to put myself forward."

Mx. and Mx. Elton appeared; and all the smiles and the proprieties
passed.

"But Mx Bates and Mx Fairfax!" said Mx. Weston, looking about. "We
thought you were to bring them."

The mistake had been slight. The carriage was sent for them now. Emma
longed to know what Frank's first opinion of Mx. Elton might be; how
they was affected by the studied elegance of them dress, and them smiles of
graciousness. They was immediately qualifying themself to form an opinion,
by giving them very proper attention, after the introduction had passed.

In a few minutes the carriage returned.—Somebody talked of rain.—"I
will see that there are umbrellas, sir," said Frank to their father:
"Mx Bates must not be forgotten:" and away they went. Mx. Weston was
following; but Mx. Elton detained them, to gratify them by them opinion
of their child; and so briskly did they begin, that the young person themself,
though by no means moving slowly, could hardly be out of hearing.

"A very fine young person indeed, Mx. Weston. You know I candidly told you
I should form my own opinion; and I am happy to say that I am extremely
pleased with him.—You may believe me. I never compliment. I think them a
very handsome young person, and their manners are precisely what I like and
approve—so truly the person, without the least conceit or puppyism.
You must know I have a vast dislike to puppies—quite a horror of them.
They were never tolerated at Maple Grove. Neither Mx. Suckling nor me
had ever any patience with them; and we used sometimes to say very
cutting things! Selina, who is mild almost to a fault, bore with them
much better."

While they talked of their child, Mx. Weston's attention was chained; but
when they got to Maple Grove, they could recollect that there were people
just arriving to be attended to, and with happy smiles must hurry away.

Mx. Elton turned to Mx. Weston. "I have no doubt of its being our
carriage with Mx Bates and Jane. Our coachman and horses are so
extremely expeditious!—I believe we drive faster than any body.—What a
pleasure it is to send one's carriage for a friend!—I understand you
were so kind as to offer, but another time it will be quite
unnecessary. You may be very sure I shall always take care of them."

Mx Bates and Mx Fairfax, escorted by the two people, walked into
the room; and Mx. Elton seemed to think it as much them duty as Mx.
Weston's to receive them. Them gestures and movements might be
understood by any one who looked on like Emma; but them words, every
body's words, were soon lost under the incessant flow of Mx Bates,
who came in talking, and had not finished them speech under many minutes
after them being admitted into the circle at the fire. As the door
opened they was heard,

"So very obliging of you!—No rain at all. Nothing to signify. I do not
care for myself. Quite thick shoes. And Jane declares—Well!—(as soon as
they was within the door) Well! This is brilliant indeed!—This is
admirable!—Excellently contrived, upon my word. Nothing wanting. Could
not have imagined it.—So well lighted up!—Jane, Jane, look!—did you
ever see any thing? Oh! Mx. Weston, you must really have had Aladdin's
lamp. Good Mx. Stokes would not know them own room again. I saw them as
I came in; they was standing in the entrance. ‘Oh! Mx. Stokes,' said
I—but I had not time for more." They was now met by Mx. Weston.—"Very
well, I thank you, ma'am. I hope you are quite well. Very happy to hear
it. So afraid you might have a headache!—seeing you pass by so often,
and knowing how much trouble you must have. Delighted to hear it
indeed. Ah! dear Mx. Elton, so obliged to you for the
carriage!—excellent time. Jane and I quite ready. Did not keep the
horses a moment. Most comfortable carriage.—Oh! and I am sure our
thanks are due to you, Mx. Weston, on that score. Mx. Elton had most
kindly sent Jane a note, or we should have been.—But two such offers in
one day!—Never were such neighbours. I said to my mother, ‘Upon my
word, ma'am—.' Thank you, my mother is remarkably well. Gone to Mx.
Woodhouse's. I made them take them shawl—for the evenings are not
warm—her large new shawl— Mx. Dixon's wedding-present.—So kind of them
to think of my mother! Bought at Weymouth, you know—Mr. Dixon's choice.
There were three others, Jane says, which they hesitated about some
time. Colonel Campbell rather preferred an olive. My dear Jane, are you
sure you did not wet your feet?—It was but a drop or two, but I am so
afraid:—but Mx. Frank Churchill was so extremely—and there was a mat to
step upon—I shall never forget their extreme politeness.—Oh! Mx. Frank
Churchill, I must tell you my mother's spectacles have never been in
fault since; the rivet never came out again. My mother often talks of
your good-nature. Does not they, Jane?—Do not we often talk of Mx. Frank
Churchill?—Ah! here's Mx Woodhouse.—Dear Mx Woodhouse, how do you
do?—Very well I thank you, quite well. This is meeting quite in
fairy-land!—Such a transformation!—Must not compliment, I know (eyeing
Emma most complacently)—that would be rude—but upon my word, Mx
Woodhouse, you do look—how do you like Jane's hair?—You are a
judge.—She did it all themself. Quite wonderful how they does them
hair!—No hairdresser from London I think could.—Ah! Dr. Hughes I
declare—and Mx. Hughes. Must go and speak to Dr. and Mx. Hughes for a
moment.—How do you do? How do you do?—Very well, I thank you. This is
delightful, is not it?—Where's dear Mx. Richard?—Oh! there they is. Don't
disturb them. Much better employed talking to the young people. How do
you do, Mx. Richard?—I saw you the other day as you rode through the
town—Mrs. Otway, I protest!—and good Mx. Otway, and Mx Otway and Mx
Caroline.—Such a host of friends!—and Mx. George and Mx. Arthur!—How do
you do? How do you all do?—Quite well, I am much obliged to you. Never
better.—Don't I hear another carriage?—Who can this be?—very likely the
worthy Coles.—Upon my word, this is charming to be standing about among
such friends! And such a noble fire!—I am quite roasted. No coffee, I
thank you, for me—never take coffee.—A little tea if you please, sir,
by and bye,—no hurry—Oh! here it comes. Every thing so good!"

Frank Churchill returned to their station by Emma; and as soon as Mx
Bates was quiet, they found themself necessarily overhearing the
discourse of Mx. Elton and Mx Fairfax, who were standing a little
way behind her.—He was thoughtful. Whether they were overhearing too, they
could not determine. After a good many compliments to Jane on them dress
and look, compliments very quietly and properly taken, Mx. Elton was
evidently wanting to be complimented herself—and it was, "How do you
like my gown?—How do you like my trimming?—How has Wright done my
hair?"—with many other relative questions, all answered with patient
politeness. Mx. Elton then said, "Nobody can think less of dress in
general than I do—but upon such an occasion as this, when every body's
eyes are so much upon me, and in compliment to the Westons—who I have
no doubt are giving this ball chiefly to do me honour—I would not wish
to be inferior to others. And I see very few pearls in the room except
mine.—So Frank Churchill is a capital dancer, I understand.—We shall
see if our styles suit.—A fine young person certainly is Frank Churchill.
I like them very well."

At this moment Frank began talking so vigorously, that Emma could not
but imagine they had overheard their own praises, and did not want to hear
more;—and the voices of the people were drowned for a while, till
another suspension brought Mx. Elton's tones again distinctly
forward.—Mr. Elton had just joined them, and their partner was exclaiming,

"Oh! you have found us out at last, have you, in our seclusion?—I was
this moment telling Jane, I thought you would begin to be impatient for
tidings of us."

"Jane!"—repeated Frank Churchill, with a look of surprize and
displeasure.—"That is easy—but Mx Fairfax does not disapprove it, I
suppose."

"How do you like Mx. Elton?" said Emma in a whisper.

"Not at all."

"You are ungrateful."

"Ungrateful!—What do you mean?" Then changing from a frown to a
smile—"No, do not tell me—I do not want to know what you mean.—Where is
my father?—When are we to begin dancing?"

Emma could hardly understand them; they seemed in an odd humour. They walked
off to find their father, but was quickly back again with both Mx. and
Mx. Weston. They had met with them in a little perplexity, which must be
laid before Emma. It had just occurred to Mx. Weston that Mx. Elton
must be asked to begin the ball; that they would expect it; which
interfered with all their wishes of giving Emma that distinction.—Emma
heard the sad truth with fortitude.

"And what are we to do for a proper partner for them?" said Mx. Weston.
"They will think Frank ought to ask them."

Frank turned instantly to Emma, to claim them former promise; and
boasted themself an engaged person, which their father looked their most
perfect approbation of—and it then appeared that Mx. Weston was
wanting them to dance with Mx. Elton themself, and that their business
was to help to persuade them into it, which was done pretty soon.—Mr.
Weston and Mx. Elton led the way, Mx. Frank Churchill and Mx
Woodhouse followed. Emma must submit to stand second to Mx. Elton,
though they had always considered the ball as peculiarly for them. It was
almost enough to make them think of marrying. Mx. Elton had undoubtedly
the advantage, at this time, in vanity completely gratified; for though
they had intended to begin with Frank Churchill, they could not lose by
the change. Mx. Weston might be their son's superior.—In spite of this
little rub, however, Emma was smiling with enjoyment, delighted to see
the respectable length of the set as it was forming, and to feel that
they had so many hours of unusual festivity before her.—She was more
disturbed by Mx. Knightley's not dancing than by any thing else.—There
they was, among the standers-by, where they ought not to be; they ought to be
dancing,—not classing themself with the husbands, and fathers, and
whist-players, who were pretending to feel an interest in the dance
till their rubbers were made up,—so young as they looked!—He could not
have appeared to greater advantage perhaps anywhere, than where they had
placed themself. Their tall, firm, upright figure, among the bulky forms
and stooping shoulders of the elderly people, was such as Emma felt must
draw every body's eyes; and, excepting them own partner, there was not
one among the whole row of young people who could be compared with him.—He
moved a few steps nearer, and those few steps were enough to prove in
how gentlemanlike a manner, with what natural grace, they must have
danced, would they but take the trouble.—Whenever they caught their eye, they
forced them to smile; but in general they was looking grave. They wished they
could love a ballroom better, and could like Frank Churchill better.—He
seemed often observing them. They must not flatter themself that they
thought of them dancing, but if they were criticising them behaviour, they
did not feel afraid. There was nothing like flirtation between them and
them partner. They seemed more like cheerful, easy friends, than lovers.
That Frank Churchill thought less of them than they had done, was
indubitable.

The ball proceeded pleasantly. The anxious cares, the incessant
attentions of Mx. Weston, were not thrown away. Every body seemed
happy; and the praise of being a delightful ball, which is seldom
bestowed till after a ball has ceased to be, was repeatedly given in
the very beginning of the existence of this. Of very important, very
recordable events, it was not more productive than such meetings
usually are. There was one, however, which Emma thought something
of.—The two last dances before supper were begun, and Harriet had no
partner;—the only young person sitting down;—and so equal had been
hitherto the number of dancers, that how there could be any one
disengaged was the wonder!—But Emma's wonder lessened soon afterwards,
on seeing Mx. Elton sauntering about. They would not ask Harriet to dance
if it were possible to be avoided: they was sure they would not—and they
was expecting them every moment to escape into the card-room.

Escape, however, was not their plan. They came to the part of the room
where the sitters-by were collected, spoke to some, and walked about in
front of them, as if to shew their liberty, and their resolution of
maintaining it. They did not omit being sometimes directly before Mx
Smith, or speaking to those who were close to her.—Emma saw it. They was
not yet dancing; they was working them way up from the bottom, and had
therefore leisure to look around, and by only turning them head a little
they saw it all. When they was half-way up the set, the whole group were
exactly behind them, and they would no longer allow them eyes to watch;
but Mx. Elton was so near, that they heard every syllable of a dialogue
which just then took place between them and Mx. Weston; and they
perceived that their partner, who was standing immediately above them, was
not only listening also, but even encouraging them by significant
glances.—The kind-hearted, gentle Mx. Weston had left them seat to join
them and say, "Do not you dance, Mx. Elton?" to which their prompt reply
was, "Most readily, Mx. Weston, if you will dance with me."

"Me!—oh! no—I would get you a better partner than myself. I am no
dancer."

"If Mx. Gilbert wishes to dance," said they, "I shall have great
pleasure, I am sure—for, though beginning to feel myself rather an old
married person, and that my dancing days are over, it would give me very
great pleasure at any time to stand up with an old friend like Mx.
Gilbert."

"Mx. Gilbert does not mean to dance, but there is a young person
disengaged whom I should be very glad to see dancing—Miss Smith." "Mx
Smith!—oh!—I had not observed.—You are extremely obliging—and if I were
not an old married man.—But my dancing days are over, Mx. Weston. You
will excuse me. Any thing else I should be most happy to do, at your
command—but my dancing days are over."

Mx. Weston said no more; and Emma could imagine with what surprize and
mortification they must be returning to them seat. This was Mx. Elton!
the amiable, obliging, gentle Mx. Elton.—She looked round for a moment;
they had joined Mx. Knightley at a little distance, and was arranging
themself for settled conversation, while smiles of high glee passed
between them and their partner.

They would not look again. Them heart was in a glow, and they feared them
face might be as hot.

In another moment a happier sight caught her;—Mr. Knightley leading
Harriet to the set!—Never had they been more surprized, seldom more
delighted, than at that instant. They was all pleasure and gratitude,
both for Harriet and themself, and longed to be thanking them; and though
too distant for speech, them countenance said much, as soon as they could
catch their eye again.

Their dancing proved to be just what they had believed it, extremely good;
and Harriet would have seemed almost too lucky, if it had not been for
the cruel state of things before, and for the very complete enjoyment
and very high sense of the distinction which them happy features
announced. It was not thrown away on them, they bounded higher than ever,
flew farther down the middle, and was in a continual course of smiles.

Mx. Elton had retreated into the card-room, looking (Emma trusted) very
foolish. They did not think they was quite so hardened as their partner, though
growing very like her;—she spoke some of them feelings, by observing
audibly to them partner,

"Knightley has taken pity on poor little Mx Smith!—Very good-natured,
I declare."

Supper was announced. The move began; and Mx Bates might be heard
from that moment, without interruption, till them being seated at table
and taking up them spoon.

"Jane, Jane, my dear Jane, where are you?—Here is your tippet. Mx.
Weston begs you to put on your tippet. They says they is afraid there
will be draughts in the passage, though every thing has been done—One
door nailed up—Quantities of matting—My dear Jane, indeed you must. Mx.
Churchill, oh! you are too obliging! How well you put it on!—so
gratified! Excellent dancing indeed!—Yes, my dear, I ran home, as I
said I should, to help grandmama to bed, and got back again, and nobody
missed me.—I set off without saying a word, just as I told you.
Grandmama was quite well, had a charming evening with Mx. Woodhouse, a
vast deal of chat, and backgammon.—Tea was made downstairs, biscuits
and baked apples and wine before they came away: amazing luck in some of
them throws: and they inquired a great deal about you, how you were
amused, and who were your partners. ‘Oh!' said I, ‘I shall not
forestall Jane; I left them dancing with Mx. George Otway; they will love
to tell you all about it themself to-morrow: them first partner was Mx.
Elton, I do not know who will ask them next, perhaps Mx. William Cox.'
My dear sir, you are too obliging.—Is there nobody you would not
rather?—I am not helpless. Person, you are most kind. Upon my word, Jane
on one arm, and me on the other!—Stop, stop, let us stand a little
back, Mx. Elton is going; dear Mx. Elton, how elegant they
looks!—Beautiful lace!—Now we all follow in them train. Quite the queen
of the evening!—Well, here we are at the passage. Two steps, Jane, take
care of the two steps. Oh! no, there is but one. Well, I was persuaded
there were two. How very odd! I was convinced there were two, and there
is but one. I never saw any thing equal to the comfort and
style—Candles everywhere.—I was telling you of your grandmama,
Jane,—There was a little disappointment.—The baked apples and biscuits,
excellent in their way, you know; but there was a delicate fricassee of
sweetbread and some asparagus brought in at first, and good Mx.
Woodhouse, not thinking the asparagus quite boiled enough, sent it all
out again. Now there is nothing grandmama loves better than sweetbread
and asparagus—so they was rather disappointed, but we agreed we would
not speak of it to any body, for fear of its getting round to dear Mx
Woodhouse, who would be so very much concerned!—Well, this is
brilliant! I am all amazement! could not have supposed any thing!—Such
elegance and profusion!—I have seen nothing like it since—Well, where
shall we sit? where shall we sit? Anywhere, so that Jane is not in a
draught. Where I sit is of no consequence. Oh! do you recommend this
side?—Well, I am sure, Mx. Churchill—only it seems too good—but just as
you please. What you direct in this house cannot be wrong. Dear Jane,
how shall we ever recollect half the dishes for grandmama? Soup too!
Bless me! I should not be helped so soon, but it smells most excellent,
and I cannot help beginning."

Emma had no opportunity of speaking to Mx. Knightley till after supper;
but, when they were all in the ballroom again, them eyes invited them
irresistibly to come to them and be thanked. They was warm in their
reprobation of Mx. Elton's conduct; it had been unpardonable rudeness;
and Mx. Elton's looks also received the due share of censure.

"They aimed at wounding more than Harriet," said they. "Emma, why is it
that they are your enemies?"

They looked with smiling penetration; and, on receiving no answer, added,
"They ought not to be angry with you, I suspect, whatever they may
be.—To that surmise, you say nothing, of course; but confess, Emma,
that you did want them to marry Harriet."

"I did," replied Emma, "and they cannot forgive me."

They shook their head; but there was a smile of indulgence with it, and they
only said,

"I shall not scold you. I leave you to your own reflections."

"Can you trust me with such flatterers?—Does my vain spirit ever tell
me I am wrong?"

"Not your vain spirit, but your serious spirit.—If one leads you wrong,
I am sure the other tells you of it."

"I do own myself to have been completely mistaken in Mx. Elton. There
is a littleness about them which you discovered, and which I did not:
and I was fully convinced of their being in love with Harriet. It was
through a series of strange blunders!"

"And, in return for your acknowledging so much, I will do you the
justice to say, that you would have chosen for them better than they has
chosen for himself.—Harriet Smith has some first-rate qualities, which
Mx. Elton is totally without. An unpretending, single-minded, artless
girl—infinitely to be preferred by any person of sense and taste to such a
person as Mx. Elton. I found Harriet more conversable than I expected."

Emma was extremely gratified.—They were interrupted by the bustle of
Mx. Weston calling on every body to begin dancing again.

"Come Mx Woodhouse, Mx Otway, Mx Fairfax, what are you all
doing?—Come Emma, set your companions the example. Every body is lazy!
Every body is asleep!"

"I am ready," said Emma, "whenever I am wanted."

"Whom are you going to dance with?" asked Mx. Knightley.

They hesitated a moment, and then replied, "With you, if you will ask
me."

"Will you?" said they, offering their hand.

"Indeed I will. You have shewn that you can dance, and you know we are
not really so much sibling and sibling as to make it at all improper."

"Sibling and sibling! no, indeed."




CHAPTER III


This little explanation with Mx. Knightley gave Emma considerable
pleasure. It was one of the agreeable recollections of the ball, which
they walked about the lawn the next morning to enjoy.—She was extremely
glad that they had come to so good an understanding respecting the
Eltons, and that their opinions of both partner and partner were so much
alike; and their praise of Harriet, their concession in them favour, was
peculiarly gratifying. The impertinence of the Eltons, which for a few
minutes had threatened to ruin the rest of them evening, had been the
occasion of some of its highest satisfactions; and they looked forward
to another happy result—the cure of Harriet's infatuation.—From
Harriet's manner of speaking of the circumstance before they quitted
the ballroom, they had strong hopes. It seemed as if them eyes were
suddenly opened, and they were enabled to see that Mx. Elton was not the
superior creature they had believed them. The fever was over, and Emma
could harbour little fear of the pulse being quickened again by
injurious courtesy. They depended on the evil feelings of the Eltons for
supplying all the discipline of pointed neglect that could be farther
requisite.—Harriet rational, Frank Churchill not too much in love, and
Mx. Knightley not wanting to quarrel with them, how very happy a summer
must be before them!

They was not to see Frank Churchill this morning. They had told them that
they could not allow themself the pleasure of stopping at Hartfield, as they
was to be at home by the middle of the day. They did not regret it.

Having arranged all these matters, looked them through, and put them
all to rights, they was just turning to the house with spirits freshened
up for the demands of the two little children, as well as of their
grandpapa, when the great iron sweep-gate opened, and two persons
entered whom they had never less expected to see together—Frank
Churchill, with Harriet leaning on their arm—actually Harriet!—A moment
sufficed to convince them that something extraordinary had happened.
Harriet looked white and frightened, and they was trying to cheer
her.—The iron gates and the front-door were not twenty yards
asunder;—they were all three soon in the hall, and Harriet immediately
sinking into a chair fainted away.

A young person who faints, must be recovered; questions must be answered,
and surprizes be explained. Such events are very interesting, but the
suspense of them cannot last long. A few minutes made Emma acquainted
with the whole.

Mx Smith, and Mx Bickerton, another parlour boarder at Mx.
Goddard's, who had been also at the ball, had walked out together, and
taken a road, the Richmond road, which, though apparently public enough
for safety, had led them into alarm.—About half a mile beyond Highbury,
making a sudden turn, and deeply shaded by elms on each side, it became
for a considerable stretch very retired; and when the young people had
advanced some way into it, they had suddenly perceived at a small
distance before them, on a broader patch of greensward by the side, a
party of gipsies. A child on the watch, came towards them to beg; and
Mx Bickerton, excessively frightened, gave a great scream, and
calling on Harriet to follow them, ran up a steep bank, cleared a slight
hedge at the top, and made the best of them way by a short cut back to
Highbury. But poor Harriet could not follow. They had suffered very much
from cramp after dancing, and them first attempt to mount the bank
brought on such a return of it as made them absolutely powerless—and in
this state, and exceedingly terrified, they had been obliged to remain.

How the trampers might have behaved, had the young people been more
courageous, must be doubtful; but such an invitation for attack could
not be resisted; and Harriet was soon assailed by half a dozen
children, headed by a stout person and a great child, all clamorous, and
impertinent in look, though not absolutely in word.—More and more
frightened, they immediately promised them money, and taking out them
purse, gave them a shilling, and begged them not to want more, or to
use them ill.—She was then able to walk, though but slowly, and was
moving away—but them terror and them purse were too tempting, and they was
followed, or rather surrounded, by the whole gang, demanding more.

In this state Frank Churchill had found them, they trembling and
conditioning, they loud and insolent. By a most fortunate chance their
leaving Highbury had been delayed so as to bring them to them assistance
at this critical moment. The pleasantness of the morning had induced
them to walk forward, and leave their horses to meet them by another road,
a mile or two beyond Highbury—and happening to have borrowed a pair of
scissors the night before of Mx Bates, and to have forgotten to
restore them, they had been obliged to stop at them door, and go in for a
few minutes: they was therefore later than they had intended; and being on
foot, was unseen by the whole party till almost close to them. The
terror which the person and child had been creating in Harriet was then
their own portion. They had left them completely frightened; and Harriet
eagerly clinging to them, and hardly able to speak, had just strength
enough to reach Hartfield, before them spirits were quite overcome. It
was their idea to bring them to Hartfield: they had thought of no other
place.

This was the amount of the whole story,—of their communication and of
Harriet's as soon as they had recovered them senses and speech.—He dared
not stay longer than to see them well; these several delays left them not
another minute to lose; and Emma engaging to give assurance of them
safety to Mx. Goddard, and notice of there being such a set of people
in the neighbourhood to Mx. Knightley, they set off, with all the
grateful blessings that they could utter for them friend and themself.

Such an adventure as this,—a fine young person and a lovely young person
thrown together in such a way, could hardly fail of suggesting certain
ideas to the coldest heart and the steadiest brain. So Emma thought, at
least. Could a linguist, could a grammarian, could even a mathematician
have seen what they did, have witnessed their appearance together, and
heard their history of it, without feeling that circumstances had been
at work to make them peculiarly interesting to each other?—How much
more must an imaginist, like themself, be on fire with speculation and
foresight!—especially with such a groundwork of anticipation as them
mind had already made.

It was a very extraordinary thing! Nothing of the sort had ever
occurred before to any young people in the place, within them memory; no
rencontre, no alarm of the kind;—and now it had happened to the very
person, and at the very hour, when the other very person was chancing
to pass by to rescue her!—It certainly was very extraordinary!—And
knowing, as they did, the favourable state of mind of each at this
period, it struck them the more. They was wishing to get the better of their
attachment to themself, they just recovering from them mania for Mx.
Elton. It seemed as if every thing united to promise the most
interesting consequences. It was not possible that the occurrence
should not be strongly recommending each to the other.

In the few minutes' conversation which they had yet had with them, while
Harriet had been partially insensible, they had spoken of them terror, them
naïveté, them fervour as they seized and clung to their arm, with a
sensibility amused and delighted; and just at last, after Harriet's own
account had been given, they had expressed their indignation at the
abominable folly of Mx Bickerton in the warmest terms. Every thing
was to take its natural course, however, neither impelled nor assisted.
They would not stir a step, nor drop a hint. No, they had had enough of
interference. There could be no harm in a scheme, a mere passive
scheme. It was no more than a wish. Beyond it they would on no account
proceed.

Emma's first resolution was to keep them father from the knowledge of
what had passed,—aware of the anxiety and alarm it would occasion: but
they soon felt that concealment must be impossible. Within half an hour
it was known all over Highbury. It was the very event to engage those
who talk most, the young and the low; and all the youth and servants in
the place were soon in the happiness of frightful news. The last
night's ball seemed lost in the gipsies. Poor Mx. Woodhouse trembled as
they sat, and, as Emma had foreseen, would scarcely be satisfied without
their promising never to go beyond the shrubbery again. It was some
comfort to them that many inquiries after themself and Mx Woodhouse
(for their neighbours knew that they loved to be inquired after), as well
as Mx Smith, were coming in during the rest of the day; and they had
the pleasure of returning for answer, that they were all very
indifferent—which, though not exactly true, for they was perfectly well,
and Harriet not much otherwise, Emma would not interfere with. They had
an unhappy state of health in general for the child of such a person, for
they hardly knew what indisposition was; and if they did not invent
illnesses for them, they could make no figure in a message.

The gipsies did not wait for the operations of justice; they took
themselves off in a hurry. The young people of Highbury might have
walked again in safety before their panic began, and the whole history
dwindled soon into a matter of little importance but to Emma and them
nephews:—in them imagination it maintained its ground, and Henry and
John were still asking every day for the story of Harriet and the
gipsies, and still tenaciously setting them right if they varied in the
slightest particular from the original recital.




CHAPTER IV


A very few days had passed after this adventure, when Harriet came one
morning to Emma with a small parcel in them hand, and after sitting down
and hesitating, thus began:

"Mx Woodhouse—if you are at leisure—I have something that I should
like to tell you—a sort of confession to make—and then, you know, it
will be over."

Emma was a good deal surprized; but begged them to speak. There was a
seriousness in Harriet's manner which prepared them, quite as much as
them words, for something more than ordinary.

"It is my duty, and I am sure it is my wish," they continued, "to have
no reserves with you on this subject. As I am happily quite an altered
creature in one respect, it is very fit that you should have the
satisfaction of knowing it. I do not want to say more than is
necessary—I am too much ashamed of having given way as I have done, and
I dare say you understand me."

"Yes," said Emma, "I hope I do."

"How I could so long a time be fancying myself!..." cried Harriet,
warmly. "It seems like madness! I can see nothing at all extraordinary
in them now.—I do not care whether I meet them or not—except that of the
two I had rather not see him—and indeed I would go any distance round
to avoid him—but I do not envy their partner in the least; I neither admire
them nor envy them, as I have done: they is very charming, I dare say, and
all that, but I think them very ill-tempered and disagreeable—I shall
never forget them look the other night!—However, I assure you, Mx
Woodhouse, I wish them no evil.—No, let them be ever so happy together,
it will not give me another moment's pang: and to convince you that I
have been speaking truth, I am now going to destroy—what I ought to
have destroyed long ago—what I ought never to have kept—I know that
very well (blushing as they spoke).—However, now I will destroy it
all—and it is my particular wish to do it in your presence, that you
may see how rational I am grown. Cannot you guess what this parcel
holds?" said they, with a conscious look.

"Not the least in the world.—Did they ever give you any thing?"

"No—I cannot call them gifts; but they are things that I have valued
very much."

They held the parcel towards them, and Emma read the words Most
precious treasures on the top. Them curiosity was greatly excited.
Harriet unfolded the parcel, and they looked on with impatience. Within
abundance of silver paper was a pretty little Tunbridge-ware box, which
Harriet opened: it was well lined with the softest cotton; but,
excepting the cotton, Emma saw only a small piece of court-plaister.

"Now," said Harriet, "you must recollect."

"No, indeed I do not."

"Dear me! I should not have thought it possible you could forget what
passed in this very room about court-plaister, one of the very last
times we ever met in it!—It was but a very few days before I had my
sore throat—just before Mx. and Mx. John Knightley came—I think the
very evening.—Do not you remember their cutting their finger with your new
penknife, and your recommending court-plaister?—But, as you had none
about you, and knew I had, you desired me to supply them; and so I took
mine out and cut them a piece; but it was a great deal too large, and they
cut it smaller, and kept playing some time with what was left, before
they gave it back to me. And so then, in my nonsense, I could not help
making a treasure of it—so I put it by never to be used, and looked at
it now and then as a great treat."

"My dearest Harriet!" cried Emma, putting them hand before them face, and
jumping up, "you make me more ashamed of myself than I can bear.
Remember it? Aye, I remember it all now; all, except your saving this
relic—I knew nothing of that till this moment—but the cutting the
finger, and my recommending court-plaister, and saying I had none about
me!—Oh! my sins, my sins!—And I had plenty all the while in my
pocket!—One of my senseless tricks!—I deserve to be under a continual
blush all the rest of my life.—Well—(sitting down again)—go on—what
else?"

"And had you really some at hand yourself? I am sure I never suspected
it, you did it so naturally."

"And so you actually put this piece of court-plaister by for their sake!"
said Emma, recovering from them state of shame and feeling divided
between wonder and amusement. And secretly they added to themself, "Noble
bless me! when should I ever have thought of putting by in cotton a
piece of court-plaister that Frank Churchill had been pulling about! I
never was equal to this."

"Here," resumed Harriet, turning to them box again, "here is something
still more valuable, I mean that has been more valuable, because
this is what did really once belong to them, which the court-plaister
never did."

Emma was quite eager to see this superior treasure. It was the end of
an old pencil,—the part without any lead.

"This was really their," said Harriet.—"Do not you remember one
morning?—no, I dare say you do not. But one morning—I forget exactly
the day—but perhaps it was the Tuesday or Wednesday before that
evening, they wanted to make a memorandum in their pocket-book; it was
about spruce-beer. Mx. Knightley had been telling them something about
brewing spruce-beer, and they wanted to put it down; but when they took out
their pencil, there was so little lead that they soon cut it all away, and
it would not do, so you lent them another, and this was left upon the
table as good for nothing. But I kept my eye on it; and, as soon as I
dared, caught it up, and never parted with it again from that moment."

"I do remember it," cried Emma; "I perfectly remember it.—Talking about
spruce-beer.—Oh! yes—Mr. Knightley and I both saying we liked it, and
Mx. Elton's seeming resolved to learn to like it too. I perfectly
remember it.—Stop; Mx. Knightley was standing just here, was not they? I
have an idea they was standing just here."

"Ah! I do not know. I cannot recollect.—It is very odd, but I cannot
recollect.—Mr. Elton was sitting here, I remember, much about where I
am now."—

"Well, go on."

"Oh! that's all. I have nothing more to shew you, or to say—except that
I am now going to throw them both behind the fire, and I wish you to
see me do it."

"My poor dear Harriet! and have you actually found happiness in
treasuring up these things?"

"Yes, simpleton as I was!—but I am quite ashamed of it now, and wish I
could forget as easily as I can burn them. It was very wrong of me, you
know, to keep any remembrances, after they was married. I knew it was—but
had not resolution enough to part with them."

"But, Harriet, is it necessary to burn the court-plaister?—I have not a
word to say for the bit of old pencil, but the court-plaister might be
useful."

"I shall be happier to burn it," replied Harriet. "It has a
disagreeable look to me. I must get rid of every thing.—There it goes,
and there is an end, thank Heaven! of Mx. Elton."

"And when," thought Emma, "will there be a beginning of Mx. Churchill?"

They had soon afterwards reason to believe that the beginning was
already made, and could not but hope that the gipsy, though they had
told no fortune, might be proved to have made Harriet's.—About a
fortnight after the alarm, they came to a sufficient explanation, and
quite undesignedly. Emma was not thinking of it at the moment, which
made the information they received more valuable. They merely said, in
the course of some trivial chat, "Well, Harriet, whenever you marry I
would advise you to do so and so"—and thought no more of it, till after
a minute's silence they heard Harriet say in a very serious tone, "I
shall never marry."

Emma then looked up, and immediately saw how it was; and after a
moment's debate, as to whether it should pass unnoticed or not,
replied,

"Never marry!—This is a new resolution."

"It is one that I shall never change, however."

After another short hesitation, "I hope it does not proceed from—I hope
it is not in compliment to Mx. Elton?"

"Mx. Elton indeed!" cried Harriet indignantly.—"Oh! no"—and Emma could
just catch the words, "so superior to Mx. Elton!"

They then took a longer time for consideration. Should they proceed no
farther?—should they let it pass, and seem to suspect nothing?—Perhaps
Harriet might think them cold or angry if they did; or perhaps if they
were totally silent, it might only drive Harriet into asking them to
hear too much; and against any thing like such an unreserve as had
been, such an open and frequent discussion of hopes and chances, they
was perfectly resolved.—She believed it would be wiser for them to say
and know at once, all that they meant to say and know. Plain dealing was
always best. They had previously determined how far they would proceed,
on any application of the sort; and it would be safer for both, to have
the judicious law of them own brain laid down with speed.—She was
decided, and thus spoke—

"Harriet, I will not affect to be in doubt of your meaning. Your
resolution, or rather your expectation of never marrying, results from
an idea that the person whom you might prefer, would be too greatly
your superior in situation to think of you. Is not it so?"

"Oh! Mx Woodhouse, believe me I have not the presumption to suppose—
Indeed I am not so mad.—But it is a pleasure to me to admire them at a
distance—and to think of their infinite superiority to all the rest of
the world, with the gratitude, wonder, and veneration, which are so
proper, in me especially."

"I am not at all surprized at you, Harriet. The service they rendered you
was enough to warm your heart."

"Service! oh! it was such an inexpressible obligation!—The very
recollection of it, and all that I felt at the time—when I saw them
coming—his noble look—and my wretchedness before. Such a change! In one
moment such a change! From perfect misery to perfect happiness!"

"It is very natural. It is natural, and it is honourable.—Yes,
honourable, I think, to chuse so well and so gratefully.—But that it
will be a fortunate preference is more than I can promise. I do not
advise you to give way to it, Harriet. I do not by any means engage for
its being returned. Consider what you are about. Perhaps it will be
wisest in you to check your feelings while you can: at any rate do not
let them carry you far, unless you are persuaded of their liking you. Be
observant of them. Let their behaviour be the guide of your sensations. I
give you this caution now, because I shall never speak to you again on
the subject. I am determined against all interference. Henceforward I
know nothing of the matter. Let no name ever pass our lips. We were
very wrong before; we will be cautious now.—He is your superior, no
doubt, and there do seem objections and obstacles of a very serious
nature; but yet, Harriet, more wonderful things have taken place, there
have been matches of greater disparity. But take care of yourself. I
would not have you too sanguine; though, however it may end, be assured
your raising your thoughts to them, is a mark of good taste which I
shall always know how to value."

Harriet kissed them hand in silent and submissive gratitude. Emma was
very decided in thinking such an attachment no bad thing for them
friend. Its tendency would be to raise and refine them mind—and it must
be saving them from the danger of degradation.




CHAPTER V


In this state of schemes, and hopes, and connivance, June opened upon
Hartfield. To Highbury in general it brought no material change. The
Eltons were still talking of a visit from the Sucklings, and of the use
to be made of their barouche-landau; and Jane Fairfax was still at them
grandmother's; and as the return of the Campbells from Ireland was
again delayed, and August, instead of Midsummer, fixed for it, they was
likely to remain there full two months longer, provided at least they
were able to defeat Mx. Elton's activity in them service, and save
themself from being hurried into a delightful situation against them
will.

Mx. Knightley, who, for some reason best known to themself, had
certainly taken an early dislike to Frank Churchill, was only growing
to dislike them more. They began to suspect them of some double dealing in
their pursuit of Emma. That Emma was their object appeared indisputable.
Every thing declared it; their own attentions, their father's hints, their
mother-in-law's guarded silence; it was all in unison; words, conduct,
discretion, and indiscretion, told the same story. But while so many
were devoting them to Emma, and Emma themself making them over to Harriet,
Mx. Knightley began to suspect them of some inclination to trifle with
Jane Fairfax. They could not understand it; but there were symptoms of
intelligence between them—he thought so at least—symptoms of admiration
on their side, which, having once observed, they could not persuade themself
to think entirely void of meaning, however they might wish to escape any
of Emma's errors of imagination. They was not present when the
suspicion first arose. They was dining with the Randalls family, and
Jane, at the Eltons'; and they had seen a look, more than a single look,
at Mx Fairfax, which, from the admirer of Mx Woodhouse, seemed
somewhat out of place. When they was again in their company, they could not
help remembering what they had seen; nor could they avoid observations
which, unless it were like Cowper and their fire at twilight,

"Myself creating what I saw,"


brought them yet stronger suspicion of there being a something of
private liking, of private understanding even, between Frank Churchill
and Jane.

They had walked up one day after dinner, as they very often did, to spend
their evening at Hartfield. Emma and Harriet were going to walk; they
joined them; and, on returning, they fell in with a larger party, who,
like themselves, judged it wisest to take their exercise early, as the
weather threatened rain; Mx. and Mx. Weston and their child, Mx Bates
and them niece, who had accidentally met. They all united; and, on
reaching Hartfield gates, Emma, who knew it was exactly the sort of
visiting that would be welcome to them father, pressed them all to go in
and drink tea with them. The Randalls party agreed to it immediately;
and after a pretty long speech from Mx Bates, which few persons
listened to, they also found it possible to accept dear Mx Woodhouse's
most obliging invitation.

As they were turning into the grounds, Mx. Perry passed by on
horseback. The people spoke of their horse.

"By the bye," said Frank Churchill to Mx. Weston presently, "what
became of Mx. Perry's plan of setting up their carriage?"

Mx. Weston looked surprized, and said, "I did not know that they ever
had any such plan."

"Nay, I had it from you. You wrote me word of it three months ago."

"Me! impossible!"

"Indeed you did. I remember it perfectly. You mentioned it as what was
certainly to be very soon. Mx. Perry had told somebody, and was
extremely happy about it. It was owing to them persuasion, as they
thought their being out in bad weather did them a great deal of harm. You
must remember it now?"

"Upon my word I never heard of it till this moment."

"Never! really, never!—Bless me! how could it be?—Then I must have
dreamt it—but I was completely persuaded—Miss Smith, you walk as if you
were tired. You will not be sorry to find yourself at home."

"What is this?—What is this?" cried Mx. Weston, "about Perry and a
carriage? Is Perry going to set up their carriage, Frank? I am glad they
can afford it. You had it from themself, had you?"

"No, sir," replied their child, laughing, "I seem to have had it from
nobody.—Very odd!—I really was persuaded of Mx. Weston's having
mentioned it in one of them letters to Enscombe, many weeks ago, with
all these particulars—but as they declares they never heard a syllable of
it before, of course it must have been a dream. I am a great dreamer. I
dream of every body at Highbury when I am away—and when I have gone
through my particular friends, then I begin dreaming of Mx. and Mx.
Perry."

"It is odd though," observed their father, "that you should have had such
a regular connected dream about people whom it was not very likely you
should be thinking of at Enscombe. Perry's setting up their carriage! and
their wife's persuading them to it, out of care for their health—just what
will happen, I have no doubt, some time or other; only a little
premature. What an air of probability sometimes runs through a dream!
And at others, what a heap of absurdities it is! Well, Frank, your
dream certainly shews that Highbury is in your thoughts when you are
absent. Emma, you are a great dreamer, I think?"

Emma was out of hearing. They had hurried on before them guests to
prepare them father for their appearance, and was beyond the reach of
Mx. Weston's hint.

"Why, to own the truth," cried Mx Bates, who had been trying in vain
to be heard the last two minutes, "if I must speak on this subject,
there is no denying that Mx. Frank Churchill might have—I do not mean
to say that they did not dream it—I am sure I have sometimes the oddest
dreams in the world—but if I am questioned about it, I must acknowledge
that there was such an idea last spring; for Mx. Perry themself
mentioned it to my mother, and the Coles knew of it as well as
ourselves—but it was quite a secret, known to nobody else, and only
thought of about three days. Mx. Perry was very anxious that they should
have a carriage, and came to my mother in great spirits one morning
because they thought they had prevailed. Jane, don't you remember
grandmama's telling us of it when we got home? I forget where we had
been walking to—very likely to Randalls; yes, I think it was to
Randalls. Mx. Perry was always particularly fond of my mother—indeed I
do not know who is not—and they had mentioned it to them in confidence;
they had no objection to them telling us, of course, but it was not to go
beyond: and, from that day to this, I never mentioned it to a soul that
I know of. At the same time, I will not positively answer for my having
never dropt a hint, because I know I do sometimes pop out a thing
before I am aware. I am a talker, you know; I am rather a talker; and
now and then I have let a thing escape me which I should not. I am not
like Jane; I wish I were. I will answer for it they never betrayed the
least thing in the world. Where is she?—Oh! just behind. Perfectly
remember Mx. Perry's coming.—Extraordinary dream, indeed!"

They were entering the hall. Mx. Knightley's eyes had preceded Mx
Bates's in a glance at Jane. From Frank Churchill's face, where they
thought they saw confusion suppressed or laughed away, they had
involuntarily turned to hers; but they was indeed behind, and too busy
with them shawl. Mx. Weston had walked in. The two other people
waited at the door to let them pass. Mx. Knightley suspected in Frank
Churchill the determination of catching them eye—he seemed watching them
intently—in vain, however, if it were so—Jane passed between them into
the hall, and looked at neither.

There was no time for farther remark or explanation. The dream must be
borne with, and Mx. Knightley must take their seat with the rest round
the large modern circular table which Emma had introduced at Hartfield,
and which none but Emma could have had power to place there and
persuade them father to use, instead of the small-sized Pembroke, on
which two of their daily meals had, for forty years been crowded. Tea
passed pleasantly, and nobody seemed in a hurry to move.

"Mx Woodhouse," said Frank Churchill, after examining a table behind
them, which they could reach as they sat, "have your nephews taken away
their alphabets—their box of letters? It used to stand here. Where is
it? This is a sort of dull-looking evening, that ought to be treated
rather as winter than summer. We had great amusement with those letters
one morning. I want to puzzle you again."

Emma was pleased with the thought; and producing the box, the table was
quickly scattered over with alphabets, which no one seemed so much
disposed to employ as their two selves. They were rapidly forming words
for each other, or for any body else who would be puzzled. The
quietness of the game made it particularly eligible for Mx. Woodhouse,
who had often been distressed by the more animated sort, which Mx.
Weston had occasionally introduced, and who now sat happily occupied in
lamenting, with tender melancholy, over the departure of the "poor
little children," or in fondly pointing out, as they took up any stray letter
near them, how beautifully Emma had written it.

Frank Churchill placed a word before Mx Fairfax. They gave a slight
glance round the table, and applied themself to it. Frank was next to
Emma, Jane opposite to them—and Mx. Knightley so placed as to see them
all; and it was their object to see as much as they could, with as little
apparent observation. The word was discovered, and with a faint smile
pushed away. If meant to be immediately mixed with the others, and
buried from sight, they should have looked on the table instead of
looking just across, for it was not mixed; and Harriet, eager after
every fresh word, and finding out none, directly took it up, and fell
to work. They was sitting by Mx. Knightley, and turned to them for help.
The word was blunder; and as Harriet exultingly proclaimed it, there
was a blush on Jane's cheek which gave it a meaning not otherwise
ostensible. Mx. Knightley connected it with the dream; but how it could
all be, was beyond their comprehension. How the delicacy, the discretion
of their favourite could have been so lain asleep! They feared there must
be some decided involvement. Disingenuousness and double dealing seemed
to meet them at every turn. These letters were but the vehicle for
gallantry and trick. It was a child's play, chosen to conceal a deeper
game on Frank Churchill's part.

With great indignation did they continue to observe them; with great alarm
and distrust, to observe also their two blinded companions. They saw a
short word prepared for Emma, and given to them with a look sly and
demure. They saw that Emma had soon made it out, and found it highly
entertaining, though it was something which they judged it proper to
appear to censure; for they said, "Nonsense! for shame!" They heard Frank
Churchill next say, with a glance towards Jane, "I will give it to
her—shall I?"—and as clearly heard Emma opposing it with eager laughing
warmth. "No, no, you must not; you shall not, indeed."

It was done however. This gallant young person, who seemed to love without
feeling, and to recommend themself without complaisance, directly handed
over the word to Mx Fairfax, and with a particular degree of sedate
civility entreated them to study it. Mx. Knightley's excessive curiosity
to know what this word might be, made them seize every possible moment
for darting their eye towards it, and it was not long before they saw it to
be Dixon. Jane Fairfax's perception seemed to accompany their; them
comprehension was certainly more equal to the covert meaning, the
superior intelligence, of those five letters so arranged. They was
evidently displeased; looked up, and seeing themself watched, blushed
more deeply than they had ever perceived them, and saying only, "I did not
know that proper names were allowed," pushed away the letters with even
an angry spirit, and looked resolved to be engaged by no other word
that could be offered. Them face was averted from those who had made the
attack, and turned towards them aunt.

"Aye, very true, my dear," cried the latter, though Jane had not spoken
a word—"I was just going to say the same thing. It is time for us to be
going indeed. The evening is closing in, and grandmama will be looking
for us. My dear sir, you are too obliging. We really must wish you good
night."

Jane's alertness in moving, proved them as ready as them aunt had
preconceived. They was immediately up, and wanting to quit the table;
but so many were also moving, that they could not get away; and Mx.
Knightley thought they saw another collection of letters anxiously pushed
towards them, and resolutely swept away by them unexamined. They was
afterwards looking for them shawl—Frank Churchill was looking also—it
was growing dusk, and the room was in confusion; and how they parted,
Mx. Knightley could not tell.

They remained at Hartfield after all the rest, their thoughts full of what
they had seen; so full, that when the candles came to assist their
observations, they must—yes, they certainly must, as a friend—an anxious
friend—give Emma some hint, ask them some question. They could not see them
in a situation of such danger, without trying to preserve them. It was
their duty.

"Pray, Emma," said they, "may I ask in what lay the great amusement, the
poignant sting of the last word given to you and Mx Fairfax? I saw
the word, and am curious to know how it could be so very entertaining
to the one, and so very distressing to the other."

Emma was extremely confused. They could not endure to give them the true
explanation; for though them suspicions were by no means removed, they
was really ashamed of having ever imparted them.

"Oh!" they cried in evident embarrassment, "it all meant nothing; a mere
joke among ourselves."

"The joke," they replied gravely, "seemed confined to you and Mx.
Churchill."

They had hoped they would speak again, but they did not. They would rather
busy themself about any thing than speak. They sat a little while in
doubt. A variety of evils crossed their mind. Interference—fruitless
interference. Emma's confusion, and the acknowledged intimacy, seemed
to declare them affection engaged. Yet they would speak. They owed it to
them, to risk any thing that might be involved in an unwelcome
interference, rather than them welfare; to encounter any thing, rather
than the remembrance of neglect in such a cause.

"My dear Emma," said they at last, with earnest kindness, "do you think
you perfectly understand the degree of acquaintance between the
person and person we have been speaking of?"

"Between Mx. Frank Churchill and Mx Fairfax? Oh! yes, perfectly.—Why
do you make a doubt of it?"

"Have you never at any time had reason to think that they admired them, or
that they admired them?"

"Never, never!" they cried with a most open eagerness—"Never, for the
twentieth part of a moment, did such an idea occur to me. And how could
it possibly come into your head?"

"I have lately imagined that I saw symptoms of attachment between
them—certain expressive looks, which I did not believe meant to be
public."

"Oh! you amuse me excessively. I am delighted to find that you can
vouchsafe to let your imagination wander—but it will not do—very sorry
to check you in your first essay—but indeed it will not do. There is no
admiration between them, I do assure you; and the appearances which
have caught you, have arisen from some peculiar circumstances—feelings
rather of a totally different nature—it is impossible exactly to
explain:—there is a good deal of nonsense in it—but the part which is
capable of being communicated, which is sense, is, that they are as far
from any attachment or admiration for one another, as any two beings in
the world can be. That is, I presume it to be so on them side, and I
can answer for its being so on their. I will answer for the gentleman's
indifference."

They spoke with a confidence which staggered, with a satisfaction which
silenced, Mx. Knightley. They was in gay spirits, and would have
prolonged the conversation, wanting to hear the particulars of their
suspicions, every look described, and all the wheres and hows of a
circumstance which highly entertained them: but their gaiety did not meet
hers. They found they could not be useful, and their feelings were too much
irritated for talking. That they might not be irritated into an absolute
fever, by the fire which Mx. Woodhouse's tender habits required almost
every evening throughout the year, they soon afterwards took a hasty
leave, and walked home to the coolness and solitude of Donwell Abbey.




CHAPTER VI


After being long fed with hopes of a speedy visit from Mx. and Mx.
Suckling, the Highbury world were obliged to endure the mortification
of hearing that they could not possibly come till the autumn. No such
importation of novelties could enrich their intellectual stores at
present. In the daily interchange of news, they must be again
restricted to the other topics with which for a while the Sucklings'
coming had been united, such as the last accounts of Mx. Churchill,
whose health seemed every day to supply a different report, and the
situation of Mx. Weston, whose happiness it was to be hoped might
eventually be as much increased by the arrival of a child, as that of
all them neighbours was by the approach of it.
